follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'v\' in \'vie\'">v</span><span title="/&#593;&#720;/: \'a\' in \'father\'">&#593;&#720;</span><span title="\'l\' in \'lie\'">l</span><span title="/u&#720;/: \'oo\' in \'goose\'">u&#720;</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Tuvalu.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/68/En-us-Tuvalu.ogg" title="En-us-Tuvalu.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">too-<span style="font-size:90%">VAH</span>-loo</i></a> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'t\' in \'tie\'">t</span><span title="/u&#720;/: \'oo\' in \'goose\'">u&#720;</span><span title="\'v\' in \'vie\'">v</span><span title="/&#601;/: \'a\' in \'about\'">&#601;</span><span title="\'l\' in \'lie\'">l</span><span title="/u&#720;/: \'oo\' in \'goose\'">u&#720;</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">TOO</span>-v&#601;-loo</i></a>), formerly known as the <b>Ellice Islands</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesian</a> <a href="/wiki/Island_country" title="Island country">island country</a> located in the Pacific Ocean, situated in Oceania, about midway between Hawaii and Australia. It lies east-northeast of the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Cruz_Islands" title="Santa Cruz Islands">Santa Cruz Islands</a> (belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands" title="Solomon Islands">Solomons</a>), southeast of <a href="/wiki/Nauru" title="Nauru">Nauru</a>, south of <a href="/wiki/Kiribati" title="Kiribati">Kiribati</a>, west of <a href="/wiki/Tokelau" title="Tokelau">Tokelau</a>, northwest of <a href="/wiki/Samoa" title="Samoa">Samoa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wallis_and_Futuna" title="Wallis and Futuna">Wallis and Futuna</a> and north of <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">Fiji</a>. It comprises three <a href="/wiki/Reef" title="Reef">reef</a> islands and six true <a href="/wiki/Atoll" title="Atoll">atolls</a> spread out between the <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> of <a href="/wiki/5th_parallel_south" title="5th parallel south">5&#176;</a> to <a href="/wiki/10th_parallel_south" title="10th parallel south">10&#176; south</a> and <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a> of <a href="/wiki/176th_meridian_east" title="176th meridian east">176&#176;</a> to <a href="/wiki/180th_meridian" title="180th meridian">180&#176;</a>, west of the <a href="/wiki/International_Date_Line" title="International Date Line">International Date Line</a>. Tuvalu has a population of 10,640 (2012 census).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1C2012_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-1C2012-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2C2012_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-2C2012-3">[3]</a></sup> The total land area of the islands of Tuvalu is 26 square kilometres (10&#160;sq&#160;mi).\n</p><p>The first inhabitants of Tuvalu were <a href="/wiki/Polynesians" title="Polynesians">Polynesians</a>. The pattern of settlement that is believed to have occurred is that the Polynesians spread out from <a href="/wiki/Samoa" title="Samoa">Samoa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tonga" title="Tonga">Tonga</a> into the Tuvaluan atolls, with Tuvalu providing a stepping stone to migration into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polynesian_outliers" title="Polynesian outliers">Polynesian Outlier communities</a> in <a href="/wiki/Melanesia" title="Melanesia">Melanesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Micronesia" title="Micronesia">Micronesia</a>.\n</p><p>In 1568, Spanish navigator <a href="/wiki/%C3%81lvaro_de_Menda%C3%B1a_de_Neira" title="&#193;lvaro de Menda&#241;a de Neira">&#193;lvaro de Menda&#241;a</a> was the first European to sail through the archipelago, sighting the island of <a href="/wiki/Nui_(atoll)" title="Nui (atoll)">Nui</a> during his expedition in search of <a href="/wiki/Terra_Australis" title="Terra Australis">Terra Australis</a>. The island of <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a> was named Ellice\'s Island in 1819; the name Ellice was applied to all nine islands after the work of English hydrographer <a href="/wiki/Alexander_George_Findlay" title="Alexander George Findlay">Alexander George Findlay</a>. The Ellice Islands came into Great Britain\'s sphere of influence in the late 19th century, as the result of a treaty between Great Britain and Germany relating to the demarcation of the spheres of influence in the Pacific Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Each of the Ellice Islands was declared a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_protectorates" title="British protectorates">British Protectorate</a> by Captain Gibson of <a href="/wiki/HMS_Curacoa_(1878)" title="HMS Curacoa (1878)">HMS&#160;<i>Curacoa</i></a> between 9 and 16 October 1892. The Ellice Islands were administered as a British protectorate by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resident_Commissioner" title="Resident Commissioner">Resident Commissioner</a> from 1892 to 1916, as part of the <a href="/wiki/British_Western_Pacific_Territories" title="British Western Pacific Territories">British Western Pacific Territories</a> (BWPT), and then as part of the <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_and_Ellice_Islands" title="Gilbert and Ellice Islands">Gilbert and Ellice Islands</a> colony from 1916 to 1976.\n</p><p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ellice_Islands_self-determination_referendum,_1974" title="Ellice Islands self-determination referendum, 1974">referendum was held in December 1974</a> to determine whether the Gilbert Islands and Ellice Islands should each have their own administration. As a consequence of the referendum, the Gilbert and Ellice Islands colony ceased to exist on 1 January 1976, and the separate British colonies of <a href="/wiki/Kiribati" title="Kiribati">Kiribati</a> and Tuvalu came into existence. Tuvalu became fully independent within the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_realm" title="Commonwealth realm">Commonwealth</a> on 1 October 1978. On 5 September 2000, Tuvalu became the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1290" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1290">189th member of the United Nations</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" Tuvalu faces as a result of climate change</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-43"><a href="#2015_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference_(COP21)"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">2015 United Nations Climate Change Conference (COP21)</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#Filmography_and_bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Filmography and bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-48"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Tuvalu" title="History of Tuvalu">History of Tuvalu</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_history_of_Tuvalu" title="Timeline of the history of Tuvalu">Timeline of the history of Tuvalu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_Tuvalu" title="Outline of Tuvalu">Outline of Tuvalu</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-history">Pre-history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Polynesian_people" title="History of the Polynesian people">origins of the people of Tuvalu</a> are addressed in the theories regarding migration into the Pacific that began about 3000 years ago. During pre-European-contact times there was frequent canoe voyaging between the nearer islands including <a href="/wiki/Samoa" title="Samoa">Samoa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tonga" title="Tonga">Tonga</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howe_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howe-5">[5]</a></sup> Eight of the nine islands of Tuvalu were inhabited; thus the name, Tuvalu, means "eight standing together" in <a href="/wiki/Tuvaluan_language" title="Tuvaluan language">Tuvaluan</a> (compare to <i>*walo</i> meaning "eight" in <a href="/wiki/Proto-Austronesian_language#Numerals" title="Proto-Austronesian language">Proto-Austronesian</a>). Possible evidence of fire in the <a href="/wiki/Caves_of_Nanumanga" title="Caves of Nanumanga">Caves of Nanumanga</a> may indicate human occupation for thousands of years.\n</p><p>An important <a href="/wiki/Tuvaluan_mythology" title="Tuvaluan mythology">creation myth of the islands of Tuvalu</a> is the story of the <i>te Pusi mo te Ali</i> (the Eel and the Flounder) who created the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islands_of_Tuvalu" title="Islands of Tuvalu">islands of Tuvalu</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JREF_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-JREF-6">[6]</a></sup> <i>te Ali</i> (the <a href="/wiki/Bothidae" title="Bothidae">flounder</a>) is believed to be the origin of the flat <a href="/wiki/Atoll" title="Atoll">atolls</a> of Tuvalu and the <i>te Pusin</i> (the Eel) is the model for the <a href="/wiki/Coconut" title="Coconut">coconut</a> palms that are important in the lives of Tuvaluans. The stories as to the ancestors of the Tuvaluans vary from island to island. On <a href="/wiki/Niutao" title="Niutao">Niutao</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAS_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-PAS-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vaitupu" title="Vaitupu">Vaitupu</a>, the founding ancestor is described as being from <a href="/wiki/Samoa" title="Samoa">Samoa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genesis_1983_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Genesis_1983-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kennedy_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kennedy-9">[9]</a></sup> whereas on <a href="/wiki/Nanumea" title="Nanumea">Nanumea</a>, the founding ancestor is described as being from <a href="/wiki/Tonga" title="Tonga">Tonga</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genesis_1983_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Genesis_1983-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_contacts_with_other_cultures">Early contacts with other cultures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early contacts with other cultures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tuvalu_costume.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="435" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Tuvalu_costume.jpg/170px-Tuvalu_costume.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Tuvalu_costume.jpg/255px-Tuvalu_costume.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Tuvalu_costume.jpg/340px-Tuvalu_costume.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tuvalu_costume.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Tuvaluan man in traditional costume drawn by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfred_Agate" title="Alfred Agate">Alfred Agate</a> in 1841, during the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Exploring_Expedition" title="United States Exploring Expedition">United States Exploring Expedition</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanton_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stanton-10">[10]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Tuvalu was first sighted by Europeans on 16 January 1568, during the voyage of <a href="/wiki/%C3%81lvaro_de_Menda%C3%B1a_de_Neira" title="&#193;lvaro de Menda&#241;a de Neira">&#193;lvaro de Menda&#241;a</a> from Spain, who sailed past <a href="/wiki/Nui_(atoll)" title="Nui (atoll)">Nui</a> and charted it as <i>Isla de Jes&#250;s</i> (Spanish for "Island of Jesus") because the previous day was the feast of the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Holy_Name_of_Jesus" title="Feast of the Holy Name of Jesus">Holy Name</a>. Menda&#241;a made contact with the islanders but was unable to land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HEMaude59_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-HEMaude59-12">[12]</a></sup> During Menda&#241;a\'s second voyage across the Pacific he passed <a href="/wiki/Niulakita" title="Niulakita">Niulakita</a> on 29 August 1595, which he named <i>La Solitaria</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HEMaude59_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-HEMaude59-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KSDDM_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-KSDDM-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Captain <a href="/wiki/John_Byron" title="John Byron">John Byron</a> passed through the islands of Tuvalu in 1764, during his circumnavigation of the globe as captain of the <a href="/wiki/HMS_Dolphin_(1751)" title="HMS Dolphin (1751)"><i>Dolphin</i>&#160;(1751)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> He charted the atolls as <i>Lagoon Islands</i>. Keith S. Chambers and Doug Munro (1980) identified <a href="/wiki/Niutao" title="Niutao">Niutao</a> as the island that <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Antonio_Mourelle" title="Francisco Antonio Mourelle">Francisco Mourelle de la R&#250;a</a> sailed past on 5 May 1781, thus solving what Europeans had called <i>The Mystery of Gran Cocal</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KSDDM_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-KSDDM-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laumua_Kofe_1983_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laumua_Kofe_1983-15">[15]</a></sup> Mourelle\'s map and journal named the island <i>El Gran Cocal</i> (\'The Great Coconut Plantation\'); however, the latitude and longitude was uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laumua_Kofe_1983_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Laumua_Kofe_1983-15">[15]</a></sup> Longitude could only be reckoned crudely at the time, as accurate <a href="/wiki/Marine_chronometer" title="Marine chronometer">chronometers</a> only became available in the late 18th century.\n</p><p>The next European to visit was Arent Schuyler de Peyster, of New York, captain of the armed <a href="/wiki/Brigantine" title="Brigantine">brigantine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Privateer" title="Privateer">privateer</a> <i>Rebecca</i>, sailing under British colours,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeP1_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeP1-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeP2_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeP2-17">[17]</a></sup> which passed through the southern Tuvaluan waters in May 1819; de Peyster sighted <a href="/wiki/Nukufetau" title="Nukufetau">Nukufetau</a> and <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a>, which he named Ellice\'s Island after an English politician, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Ellice_(merchant)" title="Edward Ellice (merchant)">Edward Ellice</a>, the Member of Parliament for Coventry and the owner of the <i>Rebecca</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s cargo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laumua_Kofe_1983_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Laumua_Kofe_1983-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HEMaude86_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-HEMaude86-18">[18]</a></sup> The name Ellice was applied to all nine islands after the work of English hydrographer <a href="/wiki/Alexander_George_Findlay" title="Alexander George Findlay">Alexander George Findlay</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1820, the Russian explorer <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Lazarev" title="Mikhail Lazarev">Mikhail Lazarev</a> visited Nukufetau as commander of the <i><a href="/wiki/Mirny_(sloop-of-war)" title="Mirny (sloop-of-war)">Mirny</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laumua_Kofe_1983_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Laumua_Kofe_1983-15">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Louis_Isidore_Duperrey" title="Louis Isidore Duperrey">Louis Isidore Duperrey</a>, captain of <a href="/wiki/French_ship_Astrolabe_(1811)" title="French ship Astrolabe (1811)"><i>La Coquille</i></a>, sailed past <a href="/wiki/Nanumanga" title="Nanumanga">Nanumanga</a> in May 1824 during a circumnavigation of the earth (1822&#8211;1825).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KSDDM_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-KSDDM-13">[13]</a></sup> A Dutch expedition (the frigate <i>Maria Reigersberg</i>) found <a href="/wiki/Nui_(atoll)" title="Nui (atoll)">Nui</a> on the morning of 14 June 1825, and named the main island (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fenua_Tapu_(isle)" title="Fenua Tapu (isle)">Fenua Tapu</a>) as <i>Nederlandsch Eiland</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Whaling" title="Whaling">Whalers</a> began roving the Pacific, although they visited Tuvalu only infrequently because of the difficulties of landing on the atolls. Captain George Barrett of the <a href="/wiki/Nantucket" title="Nantucket">Nantucket</a> whaler <i>Independence II</i> has been identified as the first whaler to hunt the waters around Tuvalu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HEMaude86_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-HEMaude86-18">[18]</a></sup> He bartered coconuts from the people of <a href="/wiki/Nukulaelae" title="Nukulaelae">Nukulaelae</a> in November 1821, and also visited <a href="/wiki/Niulakita" title="Niulakita">Niulakita</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KSDDM_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-KSDDM-13">[13]</a></sup> A shore camp was established on <a href="/wiki/Sakalua" title="Sakalua">Sakalua</a> islet of <a href="/wiki/Nukufetau" title="Nukufetau">Nukufetau</a>, where coal was used to melt down the whale blubber.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TAH16_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-TAH16-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For less than a year between 1862 and 1863, Peruvian ships engaged in the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Blackbirding" title="Blackbirding">blackbirding</a>" trade combed the smaller islands of <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesia</a> from <a href="/wiki/Easter_Island" title="Easter Island">Easter Island</a> in the eastern Pacific to Tuvalu and the southern atolls of the Gilbert Islands (now Kiribati), seeking recruits to fill the extreme labour shortage in Peru.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> While some islanders were voluntary recruits, the "blackbirders" were notorious for enticing islanders on to ships with tricks, such as pretending to be Christian missionaries, as well as kidnapping islanders at gunpoint. The Rev. A. W. Murray,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> the earliest European missionary in Tuvalu, reported that in 1863 about 170 people were taken from Funafuti and about 250 were taken from Nukulaelae,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laumua_Kofe_1983_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-Laumua_Kofe_1983-15">[15]</a></sup> as there were fewer than 100 of the 300 recorded in 1861 as living on Nukulaelae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Christianity came to Tuvalu in 1861 when <a href="/wiki/Elekana" title="Elekana">Elekana</a>, a deacon of a <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregational church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manihiki" title="Manihiki">Manihiki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cook_Islands" title="Cook Islands">Cook Islands</a>, became caught in a storm and drifted for eight weeks before landing at Nukulaelae on 10 May 1861.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laumua_Kofe_1983_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-Laumua_Kofe_1983-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MGDM_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-MGDM-26">[26]</a></sup> Elekana began <a href="/wiki/Proselytism" title="Proselytism">proselytising</a> Christianity. He was trained at <a href="/wiki/Malua" title="Malua">Malua</a> Theological College, a <a href="/wiki/London_Missionary_Society" title="London Missionary Society">London Missionary Society</a> (LMS) school in Samoa, before beginning his work in establishing the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Tuvalu" title="Church of Tuvalu">Church of Tuvalu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laumua_Kofe_1983_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-Laumua_Kofe_1983-15">[15]</a></sup> In 1865, the Rev. A. W. Murray of the LMS &#8211; a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> congregationalist missionary society &#8211; arrived as the first European missionary, where he too proselytised among the inhabitants of Tuvalu. Protestantism was well established by 1878, with preachers on each island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laumua_Kofe_1983_15-7"><a href="#cite_note-Laumua_Kofe_1983-15">[15]</a></sup> In the later 19th and early 20th centuries the ministers of what became the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Tuvalu" title="Church of Tuvalu">Church of Tuvalu</a> (Te Ekalesia Kelisiano Tuvalu) were predominantly Samoans, who influenced the development of the <a href="/wiki/Tuvaluan_language" title="Tuvaluan language">Tuvaluan language</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Tuvalu" title="Music of Tuvalu">music of Tuvalu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MD_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-MD-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The islands came into Britain\'s sphere of influence in the late 19th century, when each of the Ellice Islands was declared a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_protectorates" title="British protectorates">British Protectorate</a> by Captain Gibson of <a href="/wiki/HMS_Curacoa_(1878)" title="HMS Curacoa (1878)">HMS&#160;<i>Curacoa</i></a>, between 9 and 16 October 1892.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TAHNPT_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-TAHNPT-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trading_firms_and_traders">Trading firms and traders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Trading firms and traders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tuvalu_-_Location_Map_(2013)_-_TUV_-_UNOCHA.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="251" data-file-width="251" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Tuvalu_-_Location_Map_%282013%29_-_TUV_-_UNOCHA.svg/300px-Tuvalu_-_Location_Map_%282013%29_-_TUV_-_UNOCHA.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Tuvalu_-_Location_Map_%282013%29_-_TUV_-_UNOCHA.svg/450px-Tuvalu_-_Location_Map_%282013%29_-_TUV_-_UNOCHA.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Tuvalu_-_Location_Map_%282013%29_-_TUV_-_UNOCHA.svg/600px-Tuvalu_-_Location_Map_%282013%29_-_TUV_-_UNOCHA.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tuvalu_-_Location_Map_(2013)_-_TUV_-_UNOCHA.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islands_of_Tuvalu" title="Islands of Tuvalu">Islands of Tuvalu</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Trading companies became active in Tuvalu in the mid-19th century; the trading companies engaged <a href="/wiki/Palagi" title="Palagi">palagi</a> traders who lived on the islands. John (also known as Jack) O\'Brien was the first European to settle in Tuvalu; he became a trader on Funafuti in the 1850s. He married Salai, the daughter of the paramount chief of Funafuti.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIC_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-TIC-29">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_Lewis_Becke" title="George Lewis Becke">Louis Becke</a>, who later found success as a writer, was a trader on <a href="/wiki/Nanumanga" title="Nanumanga">Nanumanga</a> from April 1880 until the trading station was destroyed later that year in a <a href="/wiki/Cyclone" title="Cyclone">cyclone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAG_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-DAG-30">[30]</a></sup> He then became a trader on <a href="/wiki/Nukufetau" title="Nukufetau">Nukufetau</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAG1_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-DAG1-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SON_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-SON-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M&amp;D_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-M&amp;D-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1892, Captain Davis of <a href="/wiki/HMS_Royalist_(1883)" title="HMS Royalist (1883)">HMS&#160;<i>Royalist</i></a> reported on trading activities and traders on each of the islands visited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JRdd_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-JRdd-34">[34]</a></sup> Captain Davis identified the following traders in the Ellice Group: Edmund Duffy (<a href="/wiki/Nanumea" title="Nanumea">Nanumea</a>); <a href="/wiki/Jack_Buckland" title="Jack Buckland">Jack Buckland</a> (<a href="/wiki/Niutao" title="Niutao">Niutao</a>); Harry Nitz (<a href="/wiki/Vaitupu" title="Vaitupu">Vaitupu</a>); Jack O\'Brien (<a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a>); <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Restieaux" title="Alfred Restieaux">Alfred Restieaux</a> and Emile Fenisot (<a href="/wiki/Nukufetau" title="Nukufetau">Nukufetau</a>); and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Martin_Kleis" title="Christian Martin Kleis">Martin Kleis</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nui_(atoll)" title="Nui (atoll)">Nui</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BHT_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-BHT-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM1909_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-AM1909-36">[36]</a></sup> During this time, the greatest number of palagi traders lived on the atolls, acting as agents for the trading companies. Some islands would have competing traders, while dryer islands might only have a single trader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doug_Munro_1987_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Doug_Munro_1987-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the later 1890s and into first decade of the 20th century, structural changes occurred in the operation of the Pacific trading companies; they moved from a practice of having traders resident on each island to instead becoming a business operation where the <a href="/wiki/Supercargo" title="Supercargo">supercargo</a> (the cargo manager of a trading ship) would deal directly with the islanders when a ship visited an island. From 1900, the numbers of palagi traders in Tuvalu declined; the last of them were <a href="/wiki/Fred_Whibley" title="Fred Whibley">Fred Whibley</a> on Niutao, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Restieaux" title="Alfred Restieaux">Alfred Restieaux</a> on Nukufetau,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Martin_Kleis" title="Christian Martin Kleis">Martin Kleis</a> on Nui.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM1909_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-AM1909-36">[36]</a></sup> By 1909 there were no more resident palagi traders representing the trading companies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM1909_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-AM1909-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doug_Munro_1987_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Doug_Munro_1987-37">[37]</a></sup> although Whibley, Restieaux<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARMII_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-ARMII-39">[39]</a></sup> and Kleis<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TPB12_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-TPB12-40">[40]</a></sup> remained in the islands until their deaths.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_expeditions_and_travellers">Scientific expeditions and travellers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Scientific expeditions and travellers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nukufetauman1831.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="149" data-file-width="150" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/80/Nukufetauman1831.jpg" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nukufetauman1831.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A man from the <a href="/wiki/Nukufetau" title="Nukufetau">Nukufetau</a> atoll, drawn by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Thomas_Agate" title="Alfred Thomas Agate">Alfred Thomas Agate</a> in 1841</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Exploring_Expedition" title="United States Exploring Expedition">United States Exploring Expedition</a> under <a href="/wiki/Charles_Wilkes" title="Charles Wilkes">Charles Wilkes</a> visited <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nukufetau" title="Nukufetau">Nukufetau</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vaitupu" title="Vaitupu">Vaitupu</a> in 1841.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> During this expedition, engraver and illustrator <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Thomas_Agate" title="Alfred Thomas Agate">Alfred Thomas Agate</a> recorded the dress and tattoo patterns of the men of Nukufetau.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smith_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-smith-42">[42]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1885 or 1886, the New Zealand photographer <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Andrew_(photographer)" title="Thomas Andrew (photographer)">Thomas Andrew</a> visited Funafuti<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AT2_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-AT2-43">[43]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Nui_(atoll)" title="Nui (atoll)">Nui</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AT3_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-AT3-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AT4_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-AT4-45">[45]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1890, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Louis_Stevenson" title="Robert Louis Stevenson">Robert Louis Stevenson</a>, his wife <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fanny_Van_de_Grift" title="Fanny Van de Grift">Fanny Vandegrift Stevenson</a> and her son <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Osbourne" title="Lloyd Osbourne">Lloyd Osbourne</a> sailed on the <i>Janet Nicoll</i>, a trading steamer owned by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henderson_(New_Zealand_politician)" title="Thomas Henderson (New Zealand politician)">Henderson and Macfarlane</a> of Auckland, New Zealand, which operated between Sydney and Auckland and into the central Pacific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flude_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flude-46">[46]</a></sup> The <i>Janet Nicoll</i> visited three of the Ellice Islands;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> while Fanny records that they made landfall at Funafuti, Niutao and <a href="/wiki/Nanumea" title="Nanumea">Nanumea</a>, Jane Resture suggests that it was more likely they landed at Nukufetau rather than Funafuti.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JRrls_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-JRrls-48">[48]</a></sup> An account of this voyage was written by Fanny Stevenson and published under the title <i>The Cruise of the Janet Nichol</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> together with photographs taken by Robert Louis Stevenson and Lloyd Osbourne.\n</p><p>In 1894, Count Rudolf <a href="/wiki/Festetics_family" title="Festetics family">Festetics</a> de Tolna, his wife Eila (<i>n&#233;e</i> Haggin) and her daughter Blanche Haggin visited Funafuti aboard the yacht <i>Le Tolna</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The Count spent several days photographing men and woman on Funafuti.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-QBFR_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-QBFR-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMK_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-NMK-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H-C-Fassett-Ellice-Is-1900.jpg"><img alt="1900, Woman on Funafuti, Tuvalu, then known as Ellice Islands" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="618" data-file-width="533" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/H-C-Fassett-Ellice-Is-1900.jpg/220px-H-C-Fassett-Ellice-Is-1900.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/H-C-Fassett-Ellice-Is-1900.jpg/330px-H-C-Fassett-Ellice-Is-1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/H-C-Fassett-Ellice-Is-1900.jpg/440px-H-C-Fassett-Ellice-Is-1900.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H-C-Fassett-Ellice-Is-1900.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Woman on <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a> (1900)<br/>photography by <a href="/wiki/Harry_Clifford_Fassett" title="Harry Clifford Fassett">Harry Clifford Fassett</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The boreholes on Funafuti, at the site now called <i>Darwin\'s Drill</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PDN_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-PDN-53">[53]</a></sup> are the result of drilling conducted by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_London" title="Royal Society of London">Royal Society of London</a> for the purpose of investigating the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formation_of_coral_reefs" title="Formation of coral reefs">formation of coral reefs</a> to determine whether traces of shallow water organisms could be found at depth in the <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">coral</a> of Pacific atolls. This investigation followed the work on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Structure_and_Distribution_of_Coral_Reefs" title="The Structure and Distribution of Coral Reefs">The Structure and Distribution of Coral Reefs</a></i> conducted by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> in the Pacific. Drilling occurred in 1896, 1897 and 1898.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Professor <a href="/wiki/Edgeworth_David" title="Edgeworth David">Edgeworth David</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Sydney" title="University of Sydney">University of Sydney</a> was a member of the 1896 "Funafuti Coral Reef Boring Expedition of the Royal Society", under <a href="/wiki/William_Johnson_Sollas" title="William Johnson Sollas">Professor William Sollas</a> and lead the expedition in 1897.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Photographers on these trips recorded people, communities, and scenes at Funafuti.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USydL_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-USydL-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Charles_Hedley" title="Charles Hedley">Charles Hedley</a>, a naturalist at the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Museum" title="Australian Museum">Australian Museum</a>, accompanied the 1896 expedition, and during his stay on Funafuti he collected <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrate</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnological" title="Ethnological">ethnological</a> objects. The descriptions of these were published in <i>Memoir III of the Australian Museum Sydney</i> between 1896 and 1900. Hedley also wrote the <i>General Account of the Atoll of Funafuti</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CH1_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-CH1-57">[57]</a></sup> <i>The Ethnology of Funafuti</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CH2_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-CH2-58">[58]</a></sup> and <i>The Mollusca of Funafuti</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Serle_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Serle-60">[60]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Ravenswood_Waite" title="Edgar Ravenswood Waite">Edgar Waite</a> was also part of the 1896 expedition and published <i>The mammals, reptiles, and fishes of Funafuti</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ERW_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-ERW-61">[61]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Joseph_Rainbow" title="William Joseph Rainbow">William Rainbow</a> described the spiders and insects collected at Funafuti in <i>The insect fauna of Funafuti</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WJR_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-WJR-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Harry_Clifford_Fassett" title="Harry Clifford Fassett">Harry Clifford Fassett</a>, captain\'s clerk and photographer, recorded people, communities and scenes at Funafuti in 1900 during a visit of <a href="/wiki/USS_Albatross_(1882)" title="USS Albatross (1882)">USFC <i>Albatross</i></a> when the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Fish_Commission" title="United States Fish Commission">United States Fish Commission</a> was investigating the formation of coral reefs on Pacific atolls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NARA_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-NARA-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_administration">Colonial administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Colonial administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/British_Western_Pacific_Territories" title="British Western Pacific Territories">British Western Pacific Territories</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_and_Ellice_Islands" title="Gilbert and Ellice Islands">Gilbert and Ellice Islands</a></div>\n<p>The Ellice Islands were administered as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_protectorates" title="British protectorates">British Protectorate</a> from 1892 to 1916, as part of the <a href="/wiki/British_Western_Pacific_Territories" title="British Western Pacific Territories">British Western Pacific Territories</a> (BWPT), by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resident_Commissioner" title="Resident Commissioner">Resident Commissioner</a> based in the Gilbert Islands. The administration of the BWTP ended in 1916, and the <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_and_Ellice_Islands" title="Gilbert and Ellice Islands">Gilbert and Ellice Islands</a> Colony was established, which existed until 1976.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_World_War">Second World War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Second World War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">Pacific War</a>, Funafuti was used as a base to prepare for the subsequent seaborne attacks on the Gilbert Islands (<a href="/wiki/Kiribati" title="Kiribati">Kiribati</a>) that were occupied by Japanese forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PMcQ_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-PMcQ-64">[64]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">United States Marine Corps</a> landed on Funafuti on 2 October 1942<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pacificwrecks1_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-pacificwrecks1-65">[65]</a></sup> and on Nanumea and Nukufetau in August 1943. The Japanese had already occupied <a href="/wiki/Tarawa" title="Tarawa">Tarawa</a> and other islands in what is now <a href="/wiki/Kiribati" title="Kiribati">Kiribati</a>, but were delayed by the losses at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Coral_Sea" title="Battle of the Coral Sea">Battle of the Coral Sea</a>. The islanders assisted the American forces to build airfields on <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanumea" title="Nanumea">Nanumea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nukufetau" title="Nukufetau">Nukufetau</a> and to unload supplies from ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LNKFK2_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-LNKFK2-66">[66]</a></sup> On Funafuti, the islanders shifted to the smaller islets so as to allow the American forces to build the airfield and to build naval bases and port facilities on <a href="/wiki/Fongafale" title="Fongafale">Fongafale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TAHMT_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-TAHMT-67">[67]</a></sup> A Naval Construction Battalion (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seabees" title="Seabees">Seabees</a>) built a seaplane ramp on the lagoon side of Fongafale islet, for seaplane operations by both short- and long-range seaplanes, and a compacted coral runway was also constructed on Fongafale,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pacificwrecks2_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-pacificwrecks2-68">[68]</a></sup> with runways also constructed to create <a href="/wiki/Nanumea_Airfield" title="Nanumea Airfield">Nanumea Airfield</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pacificwrecks3_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-pacificwrecks3-69">[69]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Nukufetau_Airfield" title="Nukufetau Airfield">Nukufetau Airfield</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pacificwrecks4_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-pacificwrecks4-70">[70]</a></sup> USN <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PT_Boat" title="PT Boat">Patrol Torpedo Boats</a> (PTs) were based at Funafuti from 2 November 1942 to 11 May 1944.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HLB_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-HLB-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The atolls of Tuvalu acted as staging posts during the preparation for the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tarawa" title="Battle of Tarawa">Battle of Tarawa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Makin" title="Battle of Makin">Battle of Makin</a> that commenced on 20 November 1943, which were part of the implementation of "Operation Galvanic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-galvanic_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-galvanic-72">[72]</a></sup> After the war, the military airfield on Funafuti was developed into <a href="/wiki/Funafuti_International_Airport" title="Funafuti International Airport">Funafuti International Airport</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Post-World_War_II_.E2.80.93_transition_to_independence"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-World_War_II_&#8211;_transition_to_independence">Post-World War II &#8211; transition to independence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Post-World War II &#8211; transition to independence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The formation of the United Nations after World War II resulted in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Special_Committee_on_Decolonization" title="United Nations Special Committee on Decolonization">United Nations Special Committee on Decolonization</a> committing to a process of decolonisation; as a consequence, the British colonies in the Pacific started on a path to <a href="/wiki/Self-determination" title="Self-determination">self-determination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TAHES_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-TAHES-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MG2_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-MG2-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1974, the ministerial government was introduced to the <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_and_Ellice_Islands" title="Gilbert and Ellice Islands">Gilbert and Ellice Islands</a> Colony through a change to the Constitution. In that year a general election was held,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E1974_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-E1974-75">[75]</a></sup> and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ellice_Islands_self-determination_referendum,_1974" title="Ellice Islands self-determination referendum, 1974">referendum was held in December 1974</a> to determine whether the Gilbert Islands and Ellice Islands should each have their own administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-N_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-N-76">[76]</a></sup> As a consequence of the referendum, separation occurred in two stages. The Tuvaluan Order 1975, which took effect on 1 October 1975, recognised Tuvalu as a separate British dependency with its own government. The second stage occurred on 1 January 1976, when separate administrations were created out of the civil service of the Gilbert and Ellice Islands Colony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TAHTito_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-TAHTito-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TPBN_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-TPBN-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Elections to the House of Assembly of the British Colony of Tuvalu were held on 27 August 1977, with <a href="/wiki/Toaripi_Lauti" title="Toaripi Lauti">Toaripi Lauti</a> being appointed Chief Minister in the House of Assembly of the Colony of Tuvalu on 1 October 1977. The House of Assembly was dissolved in July 1978, with the government of Toaripi Lauti continuing as a <a href="/wiki/Caretaker_government" title="Caretaker government">caretaker government</a> until the 1981 elections were held.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPU81_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPU81-79">[79]</a></sup> Toaripi Lauti became the first <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Tuvalu" title="Prime Minister of Tuvalu">Prime Minister</a> on 1 October 1978, when Tuvalu became an independent nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TAHES_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-TAHES-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TAHTI_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-TAHTI-80">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tuvalu became fully independent within the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a> on 1 October 1978. On 5 September 2000, Tuvalu became the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1290" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1290">189th member of the United Nations</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Tuvalu" title="Politics of Tuvalu">Politics of Tuvalu</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Government_office_building.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4272" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Government_office_building.jpg/220px-Government_office_building.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Government_office_building.jpg/330px-Government_office_building.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Government_office_building.jpg/440px-Government_office_building.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Government_office_building.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Government office building</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parliamentary_democracy">Parliamentary democracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Parliamentary democracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Tuvalu" title="Human rights in Tuvalu">Human rights in Tuvalu</a></div>\n<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Tuvalu" title="Constitution of Tuvalu">Constitution of Tuvalu</a></i> states that it is "the supreme law of Tuvalu" and that "all other laws shall be interpreted and applied subject to this Constitution"; it sets out the <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_rights" title="Bill of rights">Principles of the Bill of Rights</a> and the Protection of the Fundamental Rights and Freedoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pacliiC_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-pacliiC-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TI_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-TI-82">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tuvalu is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parliamentary_democracy" title="Parliamentary democracy">parliamentary democracy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_realm" title="Commonwealth realm">Commonwealth realm</a> with Queen <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a> serving as the country\'s <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a> and bearing the title <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monarchy_in_Tuvalu" title="Monarchy in Tuvalu">Queen of Tuvalu</a>. Since the Queen does not reside in the islands, she is represented in Tuvalu by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Governor_General_of_Tuvalu" title="Governor General of Tuvalu">Governor General</a>, whom she appoints upon the advice of the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Tuvalu" title="Prime Minister of Tuvalu">Prime Minister of Tuvalu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPU81_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-IPU81-79">[79]</a></sup> In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuvaluan_constitutional_referendum,_1986" title="Tuvaluan constitutional referendum, 1986">1986</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuvaluan_constitutional_referendum,_2008" title="Tuvaluan constitutional referendum, 2008">2008</a>, referenda confirmed the monarchy.\n</p><p>From 1974 (the creation of the British colony of Tuvalu) until independence, the legislative body of Tuvalu was called the <i>House of the Assembly</i> or <i>Fale I Fono</i>. Following independence in October 1978, the House of the Assembly was renamed the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Tuvalu" title="Parliament of Tuvalu">Parliament of Tuvalu</a> or <i>Palamene o Tuvalu</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPU81_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-IPU81-79">[79]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unicameral" title="Unicameral">unicameral</a> Parliament has 15 members, with elections held every four years. The members of parliament select the Prime Minister (who is the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a>) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Speakers_of_the_Parliament_of_Tuvalu" title="List of Speakers of the Parliament of Tuvalu">Speaker of Parliament</a>. The ministers that form the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Tuvalu" title="Cabinet of Tuvalu">Cabinet</a> are appointed by the Governor General on the advice of the Prime Minister. There are no formal political parties; election campaigns are largely based on personal/family ties and reputations.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_National_Library_and_Archives" title="Tuvalu National Library and Archives">Tuvalu National Library and Archives</a> holds "vital documentation on the cultural, social and political heritage of Tuvalu", including surviving records from the <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_and_Ellice_Islands" title="Gilbert and Ellice Islands">colonial</a> administration, as well as Tuvalu government archives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bl.uk_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-bl.uk-83">[83]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tuvalu is a state party to the following human rights treaties: the <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Rights_of_the_Child" title="Convention on the Rights of the Child">Convention on the Rights of the Child</a> (CRC); the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Elimination_of_all_forms_of_Discrimination_Against_Women" title="Convention on the Elimination of all forms of Discrimination Against Women">Convention on the Elimination of all forms of Discrimination Against Women</a> (CEDAW) and; the <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Rights_of_Persons_with_Disabilities" title="Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities">Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities</a> (CRPD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HR_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-HR-84">[84]</a></sup> Tuvalu has commitments to ensuring human rights are respected under the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Periodic_Review" title="Universal Periodic Review">Universal Periodic Review</a> (UPR), the <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goals" title="Sustainable Development Goals">Sustainable Development Goals</a> (SDGs) and the <i>Te Kakeega III &#8211; National Strategy for Sustainable Development-2016-2020</i> (TK III), which sets out the development agenda of the Government of Tuvalu. TK III includes new strategic areas, in addition to the eight identified in TK II, which are climate change; environment; migration and urbanisation; and oceans and seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TK_III_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-TK_III-85">[85]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_system">Legal system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Legal system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>There are eight Island Courts and Lands Courts; appeals in relation to land disputes are made to the Lands Courts Appeal Panel. Appeals from the Island Courts and the Lands Courts Appeal Panel are made to the Magistrates Court, which has jurisdiction to hear civil cases involving up to <a href="/wiki/Tuvaluan_dollar" title="Tuvaluan dollar">$T</a>10,000. The superior court is the <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Tuvalu" title="High Court of Tuvalu">High Court of Tuvalu</a> as it has unlimited original jurisdiction to determine the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Tuvalu" title="Law of Tuvalu">Law of Tuvalu</a> and to hear appeals from the lower courts. Rulings of the High Court can be appealed to the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Appeal_of_Tuvalu" title="Court of Appeal of Tuvalu">Court of Appeal of Tuvalu</a>. From the Court of Appeal, there is a right of appeal to Her Majesty in Council, i.e., the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Most_Honourable_Privy_Council" title="Her Majesty\'s Most Honourable Privy Council">Privy Council</a> in London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPL_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-SPL-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PACLIIct_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-PACLIIct-87">[87]</a></sup>\n</p><p>With regard to the judiciary, "the first female Island Court magistrate was appointed to the Island Court in Nanumea in the 1980s and another in Nukulaelae in the early 1990s." There were 7 female magistrates in the Island Courts of Tuvalu (as of 2007) in comparison "to the past where only one woman magistrate served in the Magistrate Court of Tuvalu."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Tuvalu" title="Law of Tuvalu">Law of Tuvalu</a> comprises the Acts voted into law by the Parliament of Tuvalu and statutory instruments that become law; certain Acts passed by the Parliament of the United Kingdom (during the time Tuvalu was either a British protectorate or British colony); the <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a>; and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Customary_law" title="Customary law">customary law</a> (particularly in relation to the ownership of land).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPL_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-SPL-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PACLIIct_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-PACLIIct-87">[87]</a></sup> The land tenure system is largely based on <i>kaitasi</i> (extended family ownership).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TvAIP2_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-TvAIP2-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Foreign relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Tuvalu" title="Foreign relations of Tuvalu">Foreign relations of Tuvalu</a></div>\n<p>Tuvalu participates in the work of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secretariat_of_the_Pacific_Community" title="Secretariat of the Pacific Community">the Pacific Community (SPC)</a> and is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Islands_Forum" title="Pacific Islands Forum">Pacific Islands Forum</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a> and the United Nations. It has maintained a mission at the United Nations in New York City since 2000. It is a member of the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Asian_Development_Bank" title="Asian Development Bank">Asian Development Bank</a>.\n</p><p>Tuvalu maintains close relations with Fiji, New Zealand, Australia (which has maintained a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_Commission" title="High Commission">High Commission</a> in Tuvalu since 2018), Japan, South Korea, Taiwan, the United States of America, the United Kingdom and the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>. It has diplomatic relations with Taiwan; the country maintains one of the two foreign embassies in Tuvalu and has a large assistance programme in the islands.\n</p><p>A major international priority for Tuvalu in the UN, at the 2002 <a href="/wiki/Earth_Summit" title="Earth Summit">Earth Summit</a> in <a href="/wiki/Johannesburg" title="Johannesburg">Johannesburg</a>, South Africa and in other international fora, is promoting concern about global warming and the possible sea level rising. Tuvalu advocates ratification and implementation of the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a>. In December 2009, the islands stalled talks on climate change at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="United Nations Climate Change Conference">United Nations Climate Change Conference</a> in Copenhagen, fearing some other developing countries were not committing fully to binding deals on a reduction in carbon emissions. Their chief negotiator stated, "Tuvalu is one of the most vulnerable countries in the world to climate change and our future rests on the outcome of this meeting."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tuvalu participates in the <a href="/wiki/Alliance_of_Small_Island_States" title="Alliance of Small Island States">Alliance of Small Island States</a> (AOSIS), which is a coalition of small island and low-lying coastal countries that have concerns about their vulnerability to the adverse effects of global climate change. Under the <a href="/wiki/Majuro_Declaration" title="Majuro Declaration">Majuro Declaration</a>, which was signed on 5 September 2013, Tuvalu has made a commitment to implement power generation of 100% renewable energy (between 2013 and 2020), which is proposed to be implemented using Solar PV (95% of demand) and biodiesel (5% of demand). The feasibility of wind power generation will be considered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MD050913_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-MD050913-91">[91]</a></sup> Tuvalu participates in the operations of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Islands_Applied_Geoscience_Commission" title="Pacific Islands Applied Geoscience Commission">Pacific Islands Applied Geoscience Commission</a> (SOPAC) and the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Regional_Environment_Programme" title="Pacific Regional Environment Programme">Secretariat of the Pacific Regional Environment Programme</a> (SPREP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPREP_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-SPREP-92">[92]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tuvalu is party to a treaty of friendship with the United States, signed soon after independence and ratified by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">US Senate</a> in 1983, under which the United States renounced prior territorial claims to four Tuvaluan islands (<a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nukufetau" title="Nukufetau">Nukufetau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nukulaelae" title="Nukulaelae">Nukulaelae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Niulakita" title="Niulakita">Niulakita</a>) under the <a href="/wiki/Guano_Islands_Act" title="Guano Islands Act">Guano Islands Act</a> of 1856.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tuvalu participates in the operations of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Island_Forum_Fisheries_Agency" title="Pacific Island Forum Fisheries Agency">Pacific Island Forum Fisheries Agency</a> (FFA)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FFA_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FFA-94">[94]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Western_and_Central_Pacific_Fisheries_Commission" title="Western and Central Pacific Fisheries Commission">Western and Central Pacific Fisheries Commission</a> (WCPFC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FWCFC_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FWCFC-95">[95]</a></sup> The Tuvaluan government, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_government" title="US government">US government</a>, and the governments of other Pacific islands are parties to the South Pacific Tuna Treaty (SPTT), which entered into force in 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPTT_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-SPTT-96">[96]</a></sup> Tuvalu is also a member of the <a href="/wiki/Nauru_Agreement" title="Nauru Agreement">Nauru Agreement</a> which addresses the management of tuna <a href="/wiki/Seine_fishing" title="Seine fishing">purse seine fishing</a> in the tropical western Pacific. The United States and the Pacific Islands countries have negotiated the Multilateral Fisheries Treaty (which encompasses the South Pacific Tuna Treaty) to confirm access to the fisheries in the Western and Central Pacific for US tuna boats. Tuvalu and the other members of the Pacific Island Forum Fisheries Agency (FFA) and the United States have settled a tuna fishing deal for 2015; a longer term deal will be negotiated. The treaty is an extension of the Nauru Agreement and provides for the US flagged purse seine vessels to fish 8,300 days in the region in return for a payment of US$90&amp;nbs p; million made up by tuna fishing industry and US-Government contributions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> In 2015, Tuvalu refused to sell fishing days to certain nations and fleets that have blocked Tuvaluan initiatives to develop and sustain their own fishery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAC_120615_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-PAC_120615-98">[98]</a></sup> In 2016, the Minister of Natural Resources drew attention to Article 30 of the WCPF Convention, which describes the collective obligation of members to consider the disproportionate burden that management measures might place on small-island developing states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PB16_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-PB16-99">[99]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In July 2013, Tuvalu signed the Memorandum of Understanding (MOU) to establish the Pacific Regional Trade and Development Facility, which Facility originated in 2006, in the context of negotiations for an Economic Partnership Agreement (EPA) between Pacific ACP States and the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>. The rationale for the creation of the Facility being to improve the delivery of aid to Pacific island countries in support of the <a href="/wiki/Aid_effectiveness" title="Aid effectiveness">Aid-for-Trade</a> (AfT) requirements. The Pacific ACP States are the countries in the Pacific that are signatories to the <a href="/wiki/Cotonou_Agreement" title="Cotonou Agreement">Cotonou Agreement</a> with the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STT_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-STT-100">[100]</a></sup> On 18 February 2016, Tuvalu signed the Pacific Islands Development Forum Charter and formally joined the Pacific Islands Development Forum (PIDF).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PIDF0316_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-PIDF0316-101">[101]</a></sup> In June 2017, Tuvalu signed the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Agreement_on_Closer_Economic_Relations" title="Pacific Agreement on Closer Economic Relations">Pacific Agreement on Closer Economic Relations</a> (PACER).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PACER1_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-PACER1-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PACER2_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-PACER2-103">[103]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defence_and_law_enforcement">Defence and law enforcement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Defence and law enforcement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_in_Tuvalu" title="Law enforcement in Tuvalu">Law enforcement in Tuvalu</a></div>\n<p>Tuvalu has no regular military forces, and spends no money on the military. Its national police force, the <i><a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_in_Tuvalu" title="Law enforcement in Tuvalu">Tuvalu Police Force</a></i> headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a>, includes a maritime <a href="/wiki/Surveillance" title="Surveillance">surveillance</a> unit, customs, prisons and immigration. Police officers wear <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Law enforcement in the United Kingdom">British-style</a> <a href="/wiki/Uniform" title="Uniform">uniforms</a>.\n</p><p>The police have a <a href="/wiki/Pacific-class_patrol_boat" title="Pacific-class patrol boat">Pacific-class patrol boat</a> (HMTSS <i>Te Mataili</i>) provided by Australia in October 1994 under the Pacific Patrol Boat Programme for use in maritime surveillance and <a href="/wiki/Fishery" title="Fishery">fishery</a> patrol and for search-and-rescue missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> ("HMTSS" stands for <i>His/Her Majesty\'s Tuvaluan State Ship</i> or for <i>His/Her Majesty\'s Tuvalu Surveillance Ship</i>.)\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Crime_in_Tuvalu" title="Crime in Tuvalu">Crime in Tuvalu</a> is not a significant social problem due to an effective criminal justice system, also due to the influence of the <i><a href="/wiki/Falekaupule" title="Falekaupule">Falekaupule</a></i> (the traditional assembly of elders of each island) and the central role of <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Tuvalu" title="Religion in Tuvalu">religious institutions</a> in the Tuvaluan community.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Administrative divisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islands_of_Tuvalu" title="Islands of Tuvalu">Islands of Tuvalu</a></div>\n<p>Tuvalu consists of six <a href="/wiki/Atoll" title="Atoll">atolls</a> and three <a href="/wiki/Reef" title="Reef">reef</a> islands. The smallest, Niulakita, is administered as part of Niutao.\n</p><p>Each island has its own high-chief, or ulu-aliki, and several sub-chiefs (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aliki" title="Aliki">alikis</a>). The community council is the <i><a href="/wiki/Falekaupule" title="Falekaupule">Falekaupule</a></i> (the traditional assembly of elders) or <i>te sina o fenua</i> (literally: "grey-hairs of the land"). In the past, another caste, the priests (tofuga), were also amongst the decision-makers. The ulu-aliki and aliki exercise informal authority at the local level. Ulu-aliki are always chosen based on ancestry. Under the Falekaupule Act (1997),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pacliiF_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-pacliiF-105">[105]</a></sup> the powers and functions of the <i>Falekaupule</i> are now shared with the <i>pule o kaupule</i> (elected village presidents; one on each atoll).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennetts_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bennetts-106">[106]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tv-map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="352" data-file-width="329" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Tv-map.png/250px-Tv-map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/95/Tv-map.png 1.5x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tv-map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of Tuvalu.</div></div></div>\n<p>Local government districts consisting of more than one islet:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Nanumea" title="Nanumea">Nanumea</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Nui_(atoll)" title="Nui (atoll)">Nui</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Nukufetau" title="Nukufetau">Nukufetau</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Nukulaelae" title="Nukulaelae">Nukulaelae</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Vaitupu" title="Vaitupu">Vaitupu</a></li></ul>\n<p>Local government districts consisting of only one island:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Nanumanga" title="Nanumanga">Nanumanga</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Niulakita" title="Niulakita">Niulakita</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Niutao" title="Niutao">Niutao</a></li></ul>\n<p>Tuvalu has <a href="/wiki/ISO_3166-2:TV" title="ISO 3166-2:TV">ISO 3166-2 codes</a> defined for one town council (Funafuti) and seven island councils. Niulakita, which now has its own island council, is not listed, as it is administered as part of Niutao.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Tuvalu" title="Demographics of Tuvalu">Demographics of Tuvalu</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Tuvalu" title="Women in Tuvalu">Women in Tuvalu</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Population_Distribution_of_Tuvalu_by_Age_Group_(2014).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="438" data-file-width="636" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Population_Distribution_of_Tuvalu_by_Age_Group_%282014%29.png/220px-Population_Distribution_of_Tuvalu_by_Age_Group_%282014%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Population_Distribution_of_Tuvalu_by_Age_Group_%282014%29.png/330px-Population_Distribution_of_Tuvalu_by_Age_Group_%282014%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Population_Distribution_of_Tuvalu_by_Age_Group_%282014%29.png/440px-Population_Distribution_of_Tuvalu_by_Age_Group_%282014%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Population_Distribution_of_Tuvalu_by_Age_Group_(2014).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Population distribution of Tuvalu by age group (2014).</div></div></div>\n<p>The population at the 2002 census was 9,561,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSD_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-CSD-107">[107]</a></sup> and the population at the 2012 census was 10,640.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1C2012_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-1C2012-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2C2012_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-2C2012-3">[3]</a></sup> The most recent census in 2017 puts the population at 11,192.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> T<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2C2012_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-2C2012-3">[3]</a></sup> he population of Tuvalu is primarily of <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesian</a> ethnicity, with approximately 5.6% of the population being Micronesian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1C2012_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-1C2012-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Life expectancy for <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Tuvalu" title="Women in Tuvalu">women in Tuvalu</a> is 68.41 years and 64.01 years for men (2015 est.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-109">[109]</a></sup> the country\'s population growth rate is 0.82% (2015 est.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-109">[109]</a></sup> The net migration rate is estimated at &#8722;6.81 migrant(s)/1,000 population (2015 est.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-109">[109]</a></sup> The threat of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming_in_Tuvalu" title="Global warming in Tuvalu">global warming in Tuvalu</a> is not yet a dominant motivation for migration as Tuvaluans appear to prefer to continue living on the islands for reasons of lifestyle, culture and identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMJB_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-CMJB-110">[110]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 1947 to 1983, a number of Tuvaluans from Vaitupu migrated to <a href="/wiki/Kioa" title="Kioa">Kioa</a>, an island in Fiji.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LNKFK_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-LNKFK-111">[111]</a></sup> The settlers from Tuvalu were granted Fijian citizenship in 2005. In recent years, New Zealand and Australia have been the primary destinations for migration or seasonal work.\n</p><p>In 2014, attention was drawn to an appeal to the New Zealand Immigration and Protection Tribunal against the deportation of a Tuvaluan family on the basis that they were "climate change refugees", who would suffer hardship resulting from the environmental degradation of Tuvalu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-WP-112">[112]</a></sup> However, the subsequent grant of residence permits to the family was made on grounds unrelated to the refugee claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PS_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-PS-113">[113]</a></sup> The family was successful in their appeal because, under the relevant immigration legislation, there were "exceptional circumstances of a humanitarian nature" that justified the grant of resident permits, for the family was integrated into New Zealand society with a sizeable extended family that had effectively relocated to New Zealand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PS_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-PS-113">[113]</a></sup> Indeed, in 2013 a claim of a <a href="/wiki/Kiribati" title="Kiribati">Kiribati</a> man of being a <a href="/wiki/Environmental_migrant#In_Asia_&amp;_the_Pacific" title="Environmental migrant">"climate change refugee"</a> under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convention_relating_to_the_Status_of_Refugees" title="Convention relating to the Status of Refugees">Convention relating to the Status of Refugees</a> (1951) was determined by the New Zealand High Court to be untenable, for there was no persecution or serious harm related to any of the five stipulated Refugee Convention grounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Permanent migration to Australia and New Zealand, such as for family reunification, requires compliance with the immigration legislation of those countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G17_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-G17-115">[115]</a></sup>\n</p><p>New Zealand has an annual quota of 75 Tuvaluans granted work permits under the <i>Pacific Access Category</i>, as announced in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAC_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-PAC-116">[116]</a></sup> The applicants register for the Pacific Access Category (PAC) ballots; the primary criterion is that the principal applicant must have a job offer from a New Zealand employer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Tuvaluans also have access to seasonal employment in the horticulture and viticulture industries in New Zealand under the <i>Recognised Seasonal Employer</i> (RSE) Work Policy introduced in 2007 allowing for employment of up to 5,000 workers from Tuvalu and other Pacific islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Tuvaluans can participate in the Australian <i>Pacific Seasonal Worker Program</i>, which allows Pacific Islanders to obtain seasonal employment in the Australian agriculture industry, in particular, cotton and cane operations; fishing industry, in particular aquaculture; and with accommodation providers in the tourism industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17"
