title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tuvaluan_language" title="Tuvaluan language">Tuvaluan language</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tuvaluan_language" title="Tuvaluan language">Tuvaluan language</a> and English are the national languages of Tuvalu. Tuvaluan is of the Ellicean group of <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_languages" title="Polynesian languages">Polynesian languages</a>, distantly related to all other Polynesian languages such as <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_language" title="Hawaiian language">Hawaiian</a>, <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_language" title="M&#257;ori language">M&#257;ori</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tahitian_language" title="Tahitian language">Tahitian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rapa_Nui_language" title="Rapa Nui language">Rapa Nui</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samoan_language" title="Samoan language">Samoan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tongan_language" title="Tongan language">Tongan</a>. It is most closely related to the languages spoken on the <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_outlier" title="Polynesian outlier">Polynesian outliers</a> in Micronesia and northern and central <a href="/wiki/Melanesia" title="Melanesia">Melanesia</a>. The Tuvaluan language has borrowed from the Samoan language, as a consequence of Christian missionaries in the late 19th and early 20th centuries being predominantly Samoan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MD_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-MD-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tuvaluan_Te_\'gana_T&#363;valu_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tuvaluan_Te_\'gana_T&#363;valu-120">[120]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tuvaluan_language" title="Tuvaluan language">Tuvaluan language</a> is spoken by virtually everyone, while a language very similar to <a href="/wiki/Gilbertese_language" title="Gilbertese language">Gilbertese</a> is spoken on <a href="/wiki/Nui_(atoll)" title="Nui (atoll)">Nui</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tuvaluan_Te_\'gana_T&#363;valu_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tuvaluan_Te_\'gana_T&#363;valu-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> English is also an official language but is not spoken in daily use. Parliament and official functions are conducted in the Tuvaluan language.\n</p><p>There are about 13,000 Tuvaluan speakers worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Media_Corporation" title="Tuvalu Media Corporation">Radio Tuvalu</a> transmits Tuvaluan language programming.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Tuvalu" title="Religion in Tuvalu">Religion in Tuvalu</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Tuvalu" title="Church of Tuvalu">Congregational Christian Church of Tuvalu</a>, which is part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reformed_tradition" title="Reformed tradition">Reformed tradition</a>, is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_church" title="State church">state church</a> of Tuvalu;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tuvalu_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tuvalu-124">[124]</a></sup> although in practice this merely entitles it to "the privilege of performing special services on major national events".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usdos_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-usdos-125">[125]</a></sup> Its adherents comprise about 97% of the 10,837 (2012 census) inhabitants of the <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelago</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tuvalu_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tuvalu-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Tuvalu" title="Constitution of Tuvalu">Constitution of Tuvalu</a> guarantees freedom of religion, including the freedom to practice, the freedom to change religion, the right not to receive religious instruction at school or to attend religious ceremonies at school, and the right not to "take an oath or make an affirmation that is contrary to his religion or belief".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Roman Catholic community is served by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mission_Sui_Iuris_of_Funafuti" title="Mission Sui Iuris of Funafuti">Mission Sui Iuris of Funafuti</a>. Other religious groups include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seventh-day_Adventist" title="Seventh-day Adventist">Seventh-day Adventist</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bah&#225;\'&#237; Faith">Bah&#225;\'&#237;</a> with, respectively, 1.4% and 1.0% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_109-3"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-109">[109]</a></sup> According to its own estimates, the Tuvalu Brethren Church has about 500 members (i.e. 4.5% of the population).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-report12_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-report12-128">[128]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya_Muslim_Community" title="Ahmadiyya Muslim Community">Ahmadiyya Muslim Community</a> has about 50 members (0.4% of the population).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The introduction of Christianity ended the worship of ancestral spirits and other deities (<a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animism</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CHrel_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-CHrel-130">[130]</a></sup> along with the power of the <i>vaka-atua</i> (the priests of the old religions). Laumua Kofe describes the objects of worship as varying from island to island, although ancestor worship was described by Rev. D.J. Whitmee in 1870 as being common practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>See <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Tuvalu" title="Health in Tuvalu">Health in Tuvalu</a>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Children_of_Niutao_Island.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1164" data-file-width="1552" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Children_of_Niutao_Island.JPG/220px-Children_of_Niutao_Island.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Children_of_Niutao_Island.JPG/330px-Children_of_Niutao_Island.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Children_of_Niutao_Island.JPG/440px-Children_of_Niutao_Island.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Children_of_Niutao_Island.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Children on <a href="/wiki/Niutao" title="Niutao">Niutao</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/History_of_Tuvalu#Education_in_Tuvalu" title="History of Tuvalu">Education in Tuvalu</a> is free and compulsory between the ages of 6 and 15 years. Each island has a primary school. <a href="/wiki/Motufoua_Secondary_School" title="Motufoua Secondary School">Motufoua Secondary School</a> is located on <a href="/wiki/Vaitupu" title="Vaitupu">Vaitupu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mapia_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-mapia-132">[132]</a></sup> Students board at the school during the school term, returning to their home islands each school vacation. <a href="/wiki/Fetuvalu_Secondary_School" title="Fetuvalu Secondary School">Fetuvalu Secondary School</a>, a day school operated by the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Tuvalu" title="Church of Tuvalu">Church of Tuvalu</a>, is on Funafuti.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FHS_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FHS-133">[133]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fetuvalu offers the <a href="/wiki/International_General_Certificate_of_Secondary_Education" title="International General Certificate of Secondary Education">Cambridge syllabus</a>. Motufoua offers the Fiji Junior Certificate (FJC) at year 10, Tuvaluan Certificate at Year 11 and the Pacific Senior Secondary Certificate (PSSC) at Year 12, which is set by SPBEA, the Fiji-based exam board.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPBEA_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-SPBEA-134">[134]</a></sup> Sixth form students who pass their PSSC go on to the Augmented Foundation Programme, funded by the government of Tuvalu. This program is required for tertiary education programmes outside of Tuvalu and is available at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_the_South_Pacific" title="University of the South Pacific">University of the South Pacific</a> (USP) Extension Centre in Funafuti.\n</p><p>Required attendance at school is 10 years for males and 11 years for females (2001).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_109-4"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-109">[109]</a></sup> The adult literacy rate is 99.0% (2002).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMF_2010_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMF_2010-135">[135]</a></sup> In 2010, there were 1,918 students who were taught by 109 teachers (98 certified and 11 uncertified). The teacher-pupil ratio for primary schools in Tuvalu is around 1:18 for all schools with the exception of Nauti School, which has a teacher-student ratio of 1:27. Nauti School on Funafuti is the largest primary in Tuvalu with more than 900 students (45 percent of the total primary school enrolment). The pupil-teacher ratio for Tuvalu is low compared to the Pacific region (ratio of 1:29).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MDG_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-MDG-136">[136]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Community Training Centres (CTCs) have been established within the primary schools on each atoll. The CTCs provide vocational training to students who do not progress beyond Class 8 because they failed the entry qualifications for secondary education. The CTCs offer training in basic carpentry, gardening and farming, sewing and cooking. At the end of their studies the graduates can apply to continue studies either at Motufoua Secondary School or the <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Maritime_Training_Institute" title="Tuvalu Maritime Training Institute">Tuvalu Maritime Training Institute</a> (TMTI). Adults can also attend courses at the CTCs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SB_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-SB-137">[137]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Tuvaluan Employment Ordinance of 1966 sets the minimum age for paid employment at 14 years and prohibits children under the age of 15 from performing hazardous work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ilab_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-ilab-138">[138]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tuvaluan_mythology" title="Tuvaluan mythology">Tuvaluan mythology</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Interior_of_a_maneapa_in_Funafuti,_Tuvalu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1552" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Interior_of_a_maneapa_in_Funafuti%2C_Tuvalu.jpg/220px-Interior_of_a_maneapa_in_Funafuti%2C_Tuvalu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Interior_of_a_maneapa_in_Funafuti%2C_Tuvalu.jpg/330px-Interior_of_a_maneapa_in_Funafuti%2C_Tuvalu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Interior_of_a_maneapa_in_Funafuti%2C_Tuvalu.jpg/440px-Interior_of_a_maneapa_in_Funafuti%2C_Tuvalu.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Interior_of_a_maneapa_in_Funafuti,_Tuvalu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Interior of a maneapa on Funafuti, Tuvalu</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The traditional buildings of Tuvalu used plants and trees from the native broadleaf forest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CHF1_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-CHF1-139">[139]</a></sup> including timber from <i>pouka</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Hernandia" title="Hernandia">Hernandia</a> peltata</i>); <i>ngia</i> or <i>ingia</i> bush (<i><a href="/wiki/Pemphis" title="Pemphis">Pemphis</a> acidula</i>); <i>miro</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Thespesia_populnea" title="Thespesia populnea">Thespesia populnea</a></i>); <i>tonga</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Rhizophora_mucronata" title="Rhizophora mucronata">Rhizophora mucronata</a></i>); <i>Fau</i> or <i>Fo fafini</i>, or woman\'s fibre tree (<i><a href="/wiki/Hibiscus_tiliaceus" title="Hibiscus tiliaceus">Hibiscus tiliaceus</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CHF1_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-CHF1-139">[139]</a></sup> Fibre is from <a href="/wiki/Coconut" title="Coconut">coconut</a>; <i>ferra</i>, native fig (<i><a href="/wiki/Ficus" title="Ficus">Ficus</a> aspem</i>); <i>fala</i>, screw pine or <i><a href="/wiki/Pandanus" title="Pandanus">Pandanus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CHF1_139-2"><a href="#cite_note-CHF1-139">[139]</a></sup> The buildings were constructed without nails and were lashed together with a plaited <a href="/wiki/Sennit" title="Sennit">sennit</a> rope that was handmade from dried coconut fibre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MG_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-MG-140">[140]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following contact with Europeans, iron products were used including nails and corrugated roofing material. Modern building in Tuvalu are constructed from imported building materials, including imported timber and concrete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MG_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-MG-140">[140]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Church and community buildings (<a href="/wiki/Maneaba" title="Maneaba"><i>maneapa</i></a>) are usually coated with white paint that is known as <i>lase</i>, which is made by burning a large amount of dead coral with firewood. The whitish powder that is the result is mixed with water and painted on the buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TP_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-TP-141">[141]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dancer,_Tuvalu_stage,_2011_Pasifika_festival.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1932" data-file-width="1106" decoding="async" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Dancer%2C_Tuvalu_stage%2C_2011_Pasifika_festival.jpg/170px-Dancer%2C_Tuvalu_stage%2C_2011_Pasifika_festival.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Dancer%2C_Tuvalu_stage%2C_2011_Pasifika_festival.jpg/255px-Dancer%2C_Tuvalu_stage%2C_2011_Pasifika_festival.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Dancer%2C_Tuvalu_stage%2C_2011_Pasifika_festival.jpg/340px-Dancer%2C_Tuvalu_stage%2C_2011_Pasifika_festival.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dancer,_Tuvalu_stage,_2011_Pasifika_festival.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Tuvaluan dancer at Auckland\'s <a href="/wiki/Pasifika_Festival" title="Pasifika Festival">Pasifika Festival</a></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_of_Tuvalu">Art of Tuvalu</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Art of Tuvalu">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Art_of_Tuvalu" title="Art of Tuvalu">Art of Tuvalu</a></div>\n<p>The women of Tuvalu use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cowrie" title="Cowrie">cowrie</a> and other shells in traditional handicrafts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATP_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-ATP-142">[142]</a></sup> The artistic traditions of Tuvalu have traditionally been expressed in the design of clothing and traditional handicrafts such as the decoration of mats and fans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATP_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-ATP-142">[142]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Crochet" title="Crochet">Crochet</a> (<i>kolose</i>) is one of the art forms practised by Tuvaluan women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNDP1_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNDP1-143">[143]</a></sup> The material culture of Tuvalu uses traditional design elements in artefacts used in everyday life such as the design of canoes and fish hooks made from traditional materials. The design of women\'s skirts (<i>titi</i>), tops (<i>teuga saka</i>), headbands, armbands, and wristbands, which continue to be used in performances of the traditional dance songs of Tuvalu, represents contemporary Tuvaluan art and design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TPwa_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-TPwa-144">[144]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2015, an exhibition was held on Funafuti of the art of Tuvalu, with works that addressed climate change through the eyes of artists and the display of <i>Kope ote olaga</i> (possessions of life), which was a display of the various artefacts of Tuvalu culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AoT_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-AoT-145">[145]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dance_and_music">Dance and music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Dance and music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Tuvalu" title="Music of Tuvalu">Music of Tuvalu</a></div>\n<p>The traditional music of Tuvalu consists of a number of dances, including <i><a href="/wiki/Fakaseasea" title="Fakaseasea">fakaseasea</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Fakanau" title="Fakanau">fakanau</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Fatele" title="Fatele">fatele</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RG_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-RG-146">[146]</a></sup> The <i>fatele</i>, in its modern form, is performed at community events and to celebrate leaders and other prominent individuals, such as the visit of the Duke and Duchess of Cambridge in September 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CJH_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-CJH-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VM_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-VM-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RE_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-RE-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TPB_012013_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-TPB_012013-150">[150]</a></sup> The Tuvaluan style can be described "as a musical microcosm of Polynesia, where contemporary and older styles co-exist".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RG_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-RG-146">[146]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_Tuvalu" title="Cuisine of Tuvalu">Cuisine of Tuvalu</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_Tuvalu" title="Cuisine of Tuvalu">cuisine of Tuvalu</a> is based on the staple of <a href="/wiki/Coconut" title="Coconut">coconut</a> and the many species of fish found in the ocean and lagoons of the atolls. Desserts made on the islands include coconut and <a href="/wiki/Coconut_milk" title="Coconut milk">coconut milk</a>, instead of animal milk. The traditional foods eaten in Tuvalu are <a href="/wiki/Pulaka" title="Pulaka">pulaka</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taro" title="Taro">taro</a>, bananas, <a href="/wiki/Breadfruit" title="Breadfruit">breadfruit</a> and coconut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CHC_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-CHC-151">[151]</a></sup> Tuvaluans also eat seafood, including <a href="/wiki/Coconut_crab" title="Coconut crab">coconut crab</a> and fish from the lagoon and ocean. A traditional food source is seabirds (<i>taketake</i> or <a href="/wiki/Black_noddy" title="Black noddy">black noddy</a> and <i>akiaki</i> or <a href="/wiki/White_tern" title="White tern">white tern</a>), with pork being eaten mostly at <i>fateles</i> (or parties with dancing to celebrate events).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennetts_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bennetts-106">[106]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Pulaka is the main source for <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>. Seafood provides <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>. Bananas and breadfruit are supplemental crops. Coconut is used for its juice, to make other beverages and to improve the taste of some dishes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennetts_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bennetts-106">[106]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 1560-square-metre pond was built in 1996 on <a href="/wiki/Vaitupu" title="Vaitupu">Vaitupu</a> to sustain <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture_in_Tuvalu" title="Aquaculture in Tuvalu">aquaculture in Tuvalu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Flying_fish" title="Flying fish">Flying fish</a> are caught as a source of food;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CH-GA_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-CH-GA-153">[153]</a></sup> and as an exciting activity, using a boat, a butterfly net and a spotlight to attract the flying fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennetts_106-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bennetts-106">[106]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Canoe_carving_on_Nanumea.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="335" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Canoe_carving_on_Nanumea.jpg/170px-Canoe_carving_on_Nanumea.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Canoe_carving_on_Nanumea.jpg/255px-Canoe_carving_on_Nanumea.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8d/Canoe_carving_on_Nanumea.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Canoe_carving_on_Nanumea.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Canoe carving on <a href="/wiki/Nanumea" title="Nanumea">Nanumea</a></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heritage">Heritage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Heritage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The traditional community system still survives to a large extent on Tuvalu. Each family has its own task, or <i>salanga</i>, to perform for the community, such as fishing, house building or defence. The skills of a family are passed on from parents to children.\n</p><p>Most islands have their own <i>fusi</i>, community owned shops similar to convenience stores, where canned foods and bags of rice can be purchased. Goods are cheaper and fusis give better prices for their own produce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennetts_106-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bennetts-106">[106]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another important building is the <i>falekaupule</i> or <a href="/wiki/Maneaba" title="Maneaba"><i>maneapa</i></a> the traditional island meeting hall,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TKII_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-TKII-154">[154]</a></sup> where important matters are discussed and which is also used for wedding celebrations and community activities such as a <i>fatele</i> involving music, singing and dancing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennetts_106-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bennetts-106">[106]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Falekaupule" title="Falekaupule">Falekaupule</a></i> is also used as the name of the council of elders &#8211; the traditional decision making body on each island. Under the Falekaupule Act, <i>Falekaupule</i> means "traditional assembly in each island...composed in accordance with the Aganu of each island". <i>Aganu</i> means traditional customs and culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TKII_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-TKII-154">[154]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport_and_leisure">Sport and leisure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Sport and leisure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuvaluan_records_in_athletics" title="Tuvaluan records in athletics">Tuvaluan records in athletics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_at_the_Pacific_Games" title="Tuvalu at the Pacific Games">Tuvalu at the Pacific Games</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_at_the_Commonwealth_Games" title="Tuvalu at the Commonwealth Games">Tuvalu at the Commonwealth Games</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_at_the_World_Championships_in_Athletics" title="Tuvalu at the World Championships in Athletics">Tuvalu at the World Championships in Athletics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_at_the_Olympics" title="Tuvalu at the Olympics">Tuvalu at the Olympics</a></div>\n<p>A traditional sport played in Tuvalu is <i><a href="/wiki/Kilikiti" title="Kilikiti">kilikiti</a></i>, which is similar to <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricket</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-155">[155]</a></sup> A popular sport specific to Tuvalu is <i>Ano</i>, which is played with two round balls of 12&#160;cm (5&#160;in) diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennetts_106-6"><a href="#cite_note-Bennetts-106">[106]</a></sup> <i>Ano</i> is a localised version of <a href="/wiki/Volleyball" title="Volleyball">volleyball</a>, in which the two hard balls made from <a href="/wiki/Pandanus" title="Pandanus">pandanus</a> leaves are volleyed at great speed with the team members trying to stop the <i>Ano</i> hitting the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-156">[156]</a></sup> Traditional sports in the late 19th century were foot racing, <a href="/wiki/Lance" title="Lance">lance</a> throwing, <a href="/wiki/Quarterstaff" title="Quarterstaff">quarterstaff</a> fencing and wrestling, although the Christian missionaries disapproved of these activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CHS_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-CHS-157">[157]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The popular sports in Tuvalu include <i>kilikiti</i>, <i>Ano</i>, association football, <a href="/wiki/Futsal" title="Futsal">futsal</a>, volleyball, <a href="/wiki/Handball" title="Handball">handball</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basketball" title="Basketball">basketball</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby union</a>. Tuvalu has sports organisations for <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Athletics_Association" title="Tuvalu Athletics Association">athletics</a>, badminton, tennis, table tennis, volleyball, football, <a href="/wiki/Basketball_in_Tuvalu" title="Basketball in Tuvalu">basketball</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Rugby_Union" title="Tuvalu Rugby Union">rugby union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Weightlifting_Federation" title="Tuvalu Weightlifting Federation">weightlifting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Powerlifting_Federation" title="Tuvalu Powerlifting Federation">powerlifting</a>. At the <a href="/wiki/2013_Pacific_Mini_Games" title="2013 Pacific Mini Games">2013 Pacific Mini Games</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuau_Lapua_Lapua" title="Tuau Lapua Lapua">Tuau Lapua Lapua</a> won Tuvalu\'s first gold medal in an international competition in the weightlifting 62 kilogram male <a href="/wiki/Snatch_(weightlifting)" title="Snatch (weightlifting)">snatch</a>. (He also won bronze in the <a href="/wiki/Clean_and_jerk" title="Clean and jerk">clean and jerk</a>, and obtained the silver medal overall for the combined event.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RNZI030913_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-RNZI030913-158">[158]</a></sup> In 2015, <a href="/wiki/Telupe_Iosefa" title="Telupe Iosefa">Telupe Iosefa</a> received the first gold medal won by Tuvalu at the <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_at_the_2015_Pacific_Games" title="Tuvalu at the 2015 Pacific Games">Pacific Games</a> in the powerlifting 120&#160;kg male division.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IG1_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-IG1-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GEMS22_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-GEMS22-160">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RNZI1107_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-RNZI1107-161">[161]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tuvalu_national_football_team_(team_picture,_2011).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="673" data-file-width="1117" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Tuvalu_national_football_team_%28team_picture%2C_2011%29.jpg/220px-Tuvalu_national_football_team_%28team_picture%2C_2011%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Tuvalu_national_football_team_%28team_picture%2C_2011%29.jpg/330px-Tuvalu_national_football_team_%28team_picture%2C_2011%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Tuvalu_national_football_team_%28team_picture%2C_2011%29.jpg/440px-Tuvalu_national_football_team_%28team_picture%2C_2011%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tuvalu_national_football_team_(team_picture,_2011).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_national_football_team" title="Tuvalu national football team">Tuvalu national football team</a> (2011)</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Football_in_Tuvalu" title="Football in Tuvalu">Football in Tuvalu</a> is played at club and national team level. The <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_national_football_team" title="Tuvalu national football team">Tuvalu national football team</a> trains at the <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Sports_Ground" title="Tuvalu Sports Ground">Tuvalu Sports Ground</a> in <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a> and competes in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Games" title="Pacific Games">Pacific Games</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_National_Football_Association" title="Tuvalu National Football Association">Tuvalu National Football Association</a> is an associate member of the <a href="/wiki/Oceania_Football_Confederation" title="Oceania Football Confederation">Oceania Football Confederation</a> (OFC) and is seeking membership in <a href="/wiki/FIFA" title="FIFA">FIFA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frew_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frew-163">[163]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_national_futsal_team" title="Tuvalu national futsal team">Tuvalu national futsal team</a> participates in the <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_at_the_Oceanian_Futsal_Championship" title="Tuvalu at the Oceanian Futsal Championship">Oceanian Futsal Championship</a>.\n</p><p>A major sporting event is the "Independence Day Sports Festival" held annually on 1 October. The most important sports event within the country is arguably the <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Games" title="Tuvalu Games">Tuvalu Games</a>, which are held yearly since 2008. Tuvalu first participated in the Pacific Games in 1978 and in the <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_at_the_Commonwealth_Games" title="Tuvalu at the Commonwealth Games">Commonwealth Games</a> in 1998, when a weightlifter attended the games held at Kuala Lumpur, Malaysia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CGFT_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-CGFT-164">[164]</a></sup> Two table tennis players attended the 2002 Commonwealth Games in Manchester, England;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CGFT_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-CGFT-164">[164]</a></sup> Tuvalu entered competitors in shooting, table tennis and weightlifting at the <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_at_the_2006_Commonwealth_Games" title="Tuvalu at the 2006 Commonwealth Games">2006 Commonwealth Games in Melbourne</a>, Australia;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CGFT_164-2"><a href="#cite_note-CGFT-164">[164]</a></sup> three athletes participated in the <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_at_the_2010_Commonwealth_Games" title="Tuvalu at the 2010 Commonwealth Games">2010 Commonwealth Games in Delhi</a>, India, entering the discus, shot put and weightlifting events;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CGFT_164-3"><a href="#cite_note-CGFT-164">[164]</a></sup> and a team of 3 weightlifters and 2 table tennis players attended the <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_at_the_2014_Commonwealth_Games" title="Tuvalu at the 2014 Commonwealth Games">2014 Commonwealth Games in Glasgow</a>. Tuvaluan athletes have also participated in the men\'s and women\'s <a href="/wiki/100_metres" title="100 metres">100 metres</a> sprint at the <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_at_the_World_Championships_in_Athletics" title="Tuvalu at the World Championships in Athletics">World Championships in Athletics</a> from 2009.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Association_of_Sports_and_National_Olympic_Committee" title="Tuvalu Association of Sports and National Olympic Committee">Tuvalu Association of Sports and National Olympic Committee</a> (TASNOC) was recognised as a National Olympic Committee in July 2007. Tuvalu entered the Olympic Games for the first time at the <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_at_the_2008_Summer_Olympics" title="Tuvalu at the 2008 Summer Olympics">2008 Summer Games</a> in Beijing, China, with a weightlifter and two athletes in the men\'s and women\'s 100 metres sprint. A team with athletes in the same events represented <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_at_the_2012_Summer_Olympics" title="Tuvalu at the 2012 Summer Olympics">Tuvalu at the 2012 Summer Olympics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TPB_2812_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-TPB_2812-165">[165]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Etimoni_Timuani" title="Etimoni Timuani">Etimoni Timuani</a> was the sole representative of Tuvalu at the <a href="/wiki/2016_Summer_Olympics" title="2016 Summer Olympics">2016 Summer Olympics</a> in the 100m event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RS16_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-RS16-166">[166]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy_and_government_services">Economy and government services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Economy and government services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Tuvalu" title="Economy of Tuvalu">Economy of Tuvalu</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:National_Bank_of_Tuvalu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4272" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/National_Bank_of_Tuvalu.jpg/220px-National_Bank_of_Tuvalu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/National_Bank_of_Tuvalu.jpg/330px-National_Bank_of_Tuvalu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/National_Bank_of_Tuvalu.jpg/440px-National_Bank_of_Tuvalu.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:National_Bank_of_Tuvalu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/National_Bank_of_Tuvalu" title="National Bank of Tuvalu">National Bank of Tuvalu</a></div></div></div>\n<p>From 1996 to 2002, Tuvalu was one of the best-performing Pacific Island economies and achieved an average real gross domestic product (GDP) growth rate of 5.6% per annum. Economic growth slowed after 2002, with GDP growth of 1.5% in 2008. Tuvalu was exposed to rapid rises in world prices of fuel and food in 2008, with the level of inflation peaking at 13.4%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMF_2010_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-IMF_2010-135">[135]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tuvalu joined the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> (IMF) on 24 June 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMF16_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMF16-167">[167]</a></sup> The IMP 2010 Report on Tuvalu estimates that Tuvalu experienced zero growth in its 2010 GDP, after the economy contracted by about 2% in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMF_2010_135-2"><a href="#cite_note-IMF_2010-135">[135]</a></sup> On 5 August 2012, the Executive Board of the IMF concluded the Article IV consultation with Tuvalu, and assessed the economy of Tuvalu: "A slow recovery is underway in Tuvalu, but there are important risks. GDP grew in 2011 for the first time since the global financial crisis, led by the private retail sector and education spending. We expect growth to rise slowly".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMF55_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMF55-168">[168]</a></sup> The IMF 2014 Country Report noted that real GDP growth in Tuvalu had been volatile averaging only 1 percent in the past decade. The 2014 Country Report describes economic growth prospects as generally positive as the result of large revenues from fishing licences, together with substantial foreign aid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMF2014_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMF2014-169">[169]</a></sup> While a budget deficit of A$0.4&#160;million was projected for 2015, the <a href="/wiki/Asian_Development_Bank" title="Asian Development Bank">Asian Development Bank</a> (ADB) assessed the budget as being A$14.3m in surplus as the result of high tuna fish licence fees. The ADB predicted that the 2% growth rate for 2015 would continue into 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IBjan16_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-IBjan16-170">[170]</a></sup> Nonetheless, Tuvalu has the smallest GDP of any sovereign nation in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMF_Groups_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMF_Groups-171">[171]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The government is the primary provider of medical services through <a href="/wiki/Princess_Margaret_Hospital_(Funafuti)" title="Princess Margaret Hospital (Funafuti)">Princess Margaret Hospital</a> on <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a>, which operates health clinics on the other islands. Banking services are provided by the <a href="/wiki/National_Bank_of_Tuvalu" title="National Bank of Tuvalu">National Bank of Tuvalu</a>. Public sector workers make up about 65% of those formally employed. Remittances from Tuvaluans living in Australia and New Zealand, and remittances from Tuvaluan sailors employed on overseas ships are important sources of income for Tuvaluans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AusAID_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-AusAID-172">[172]</a></sup> Approximately 15% of adult males work as seamen on foreign-flagged merchant ships. <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Tuvalu" title="Agriculture in Tuvalu">Agriculture in Tuvalu</a> is focused on <a href="/wiki/Coconut" title="Coconut">coconut</a> trees and growing <a href="/wiki/Pulaka" title="Pulaka">pulaka</a> in large pits of composted soil below the water table. Tuvaluans are otherwise involved in traditional subsistence agriculture and fishing.\n</p><p>Tuvaluans are well known for their seafaring skills, with the <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Maritime_Training_Institute" title="Tuvalu Maritime Training Institute">Tuvalu Maritime Training Institute</a> on <a href="/wiki/Amatuku" title="Amatuku">Amatuku</a> <a href="/wiki/Islet" title="Islet">motu</a> (island), <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a>, providing training to approximately 120 marine cadets each year so that they have the skills necessary for employment as seafarers on merchant shipping. The <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Overseas_Seamen%27s_Union" title="Tuvalu Overseas Seamen\'s Union">Tuvalu Overseas Seamen\'s Union</a> (TOSU) is the only registered trade union in Tuvalu. It represents workers on foreign ships. The Asian Development Bank (ADB) estimates that 800 Tuvaluan men are trained, certified and active as seafarers. The ADB estimates that, at any one time, about 15% of the adult male population works abroad as seafarers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADB2011_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-ADB2011-173">[173]</a></sup> Job opportunities also exist as observers on tuna boats where the role is to monitor compliance with the boat\'s tuna fishing licence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TO0315_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-TO0315-174">[174]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Government revenues largely come from sales of fishing licences, income from the <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Trust_Fund" title="Tuvalu Trust Fund">Tuvalu Trust Fund</a>, and from the lease of its "<a href="/wiki/.tv" title=".tv">.tv</a>" Internet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Top_Level_Domain" title="Top Level Domain">Top Level Domain</a> (TLD). In 1998, Tuvalu began deriving revenue from the use of its area code for <a href="/wiki/Premium-rate_telephone_number" title="Premium-rate telephone number">premium-rate telephone numbers</a> and from the commercialisation of its ".tv" Internet domain name,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conway_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Conway-175">[175]</a></sup> which is now managed by <a href="/wiki/Verisign" title="Verisign">Verisign</a> until 2021.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> Tuvalu also generates income from postage stamps by the <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Philatelic_Bureau" title="Tuvalu Philatelic Bureau">Tuvalu Philatelic Bureau</a>, and from the <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Ship_Registry" title="Tuvalu Ship Registry">Tuvalu Ship Registry</a>.\n</p><p>The Tuvalu Trust Fund was established in 1987 by the United Kingdom, Australia, and New Zealand. The value of the Tuvalu Trust Fund is approximately $100&#160;million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMF_2010_135-3"><a href="#cite_note-IMF_2010-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NB_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-NB-177">[177]</a></sup> Financial support to Tuvalu is also provided by Japan, South Korea and the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>. Australia and New Zealand continue to contribute capital to the Tuvalu Trust Fund, and provide other forms of development assistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AusAID_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-AusAID-172">[172]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_government" title="US government">US government</a> is also a major revenue source for Tuvalu. In 1999, the payment from the South Pacific Tuna Treaty (SPTT) was about $9&#160;million, with the value increasing in the following years. In May 2013, representatives from the United States and the Pacific Islands countries agreed to sign interim arrangement documents to extend the Multilateral Fisheries Treaty (which encompasses the South Pacific Tuna Treaty) for 18 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RA160513_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-RA160513-178">[178]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The United Nations designates Tuvalu as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Least_developed_country" title="Least developed country">least developed country</a> (LDC) because of its limited potential for economic development, absence of exploitable resources and its small size and vulnerability to external economic and environmental shocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unohrlls_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-unohrlls-179">[179]</a></sup> Tuvalu participates in the Enhanced Integrated Framework for Trade-Related Technical Assistance to Least Developed Countries (EIF), which was established in October 1997 under the auspices of the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organisation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> In 2013, Tuvalu deferred its graduation from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Least_developed_country" title="Least developed country">least developed country</a> (LDC) status to a <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing country</a> to 2015. Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Enele_Sopoaga" title="Enele Sopoaga">Enele Sopoaga</a> said that this deferral was necessary to maintain access by Tuvalu to the funds provided by the United Nations\'s <a href="/wiki/National_Adaptation_Programme_of_Action" title="National Adaptation Programme of Action">National Adaptation Programme of Action</a> (NAPA), as "Once Tuvalu graduates to a developed country, it will not be considered for funding assistance for climate change adaptation programmes like NAPA, which only goes to LDCs". Tuvalu had met targets so that Tuvalu was to graduate from LDC status. Prime minister Enele Sopoaga wants the United Nations to reconsider its criteria for graduation from LDC status as not enough weight is given to the environmental plight of small island states like Tuvalu in the application of the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Vulnerability_Index" title="Environmental Vulnerability Index">Environmental Vulnerability Index</a> (EVI).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RNZ230913_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-RNZ230913-181">[181]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:View_from_Hotel_room.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4272" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/View_from_Hotel_room.jpg/220px-View_from_Hotel_room.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/View_from_Hotel_room.jpg/330px-View_from_Hotel_room.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/View_from_Hotel_room.jpg/440px-View_from_Hotel_room.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:View_from_Hotel_room.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Funafuti lagoon (<i>Te Namo</i>)</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Funafuti_Conservation_Area" title="Funafuti Conservation Area">Funafuti Conservation Area</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Tuvalu" title="Public holidays in Tuvalu">Public holidays in Tuvalu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Visa_policy_of_Tuvalu" title="Visa policy of Tuvalu">Visa policy of Tuvalu</a></div>\n<p>Due to the country\'s remoteness, tourism is not significant. Visitors totalled 1,684 in 2010: 65% were on business, development officials or technical consultants, 20% were tourists (360 people), and 11% were expatriates returning to visit family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FTMN_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FTMN-182">[182]</a></sup> In 2016, the number of visitors had increased to 2,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNWTO17_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNWTO17-183">[183]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The main island of <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a> is the focus of travellers, since the only airport in Tuvalu is the <a href="/wiki/Funafuti_International_Airport" title="Funafuti International Airport">Funafuti International Airport</a> and the island has hotel facilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ecotourism" title="Ecotourism">Ecotourism</a> is a motivation of travellers to Tuvalu. The <a href="/wiki/Funafuti_Conservation_Area" title="Funafuti Conservation Area">Funafuti Conservation Area</a> consists of 12.74 square miles (33.00 square kilometres) of ocean, reef, lagoon, channel and six uninhabited islets.\n</p><p>The outer atolls can be visited on the two passenger-cargo ships, <i>Nivaga III</i> and <i>Man&#250; Folau</i>, which provide round-trip visits to the outer islands every three or four weeks. There is guesthouse accommodation on many of the outer islands.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Telecommunications_and_media">Telecommunications and media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Telecommunications and media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_in_Tuvalu" title="Telecommunications in Tuvalu">Telecommunications in Tuvalu</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_newspapers_in_Tuvalu" title="List of newspapers in Tuvalu">List of newspapers in Tuvalu</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Media_Corporation" title="Tuvalu Media Corporation">Tuvalu Media Department</a> of the Government of Tuvalu operates <i>Radio Tuvalu</i> which broadcasts from Funafuti.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pacmas_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-pacmas-185">[185]</a></sup> In 2011, the Japanese government provided financial support to construct a new AM broadcast studio. The installation of upgraded transmission equipment allows Radio Tuvalu to be heard on all nine islands of Tuvalu. The new AM radio transmitter on Funafuti replaced the FM radio service to the outer islands and freed up satellite bandwidth for mobile services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FTMN_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-FTMN-182">[182]</a></sup> <i>Fenui &#8211; news from Tuvalu</i> is a free digital publication of the Tuvalu Media Department that is emailed to subscribers and operates a Facebook page, which publishes news about government activities and news about Tuvaluan events.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Telecommunications_Corporation" title="Tuvalu Telecommunications Corporation">Tuvalu Telecommunications Corporation</a> (TTC), a state-owned enterprise, provides fixed line telephone communications to subscribers on each island, mobile phone services on <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vaitupu" title="Vaitupu">Vaitupu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nukulaelae" title="Nukulaelae">Nukulaelae</a> and is a distributor of the <a href="/wiki/Fiji_Television" title="Fiji Television">Fiji Television</a> service (<a href="/wiki/Sky_Pacific" title="Sky Pacific">Sky Pacific</a> satellite television service).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FTMN_182-2"><a href="#cite_note-FTMN-182">[182]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communications_in_Tuvalu" title="Communications in Tuvalu">Communications in Tuvalu</a> rely on satellite dishes for telephone and internet access. The available bandwidth is only 512 kbit/s uplink, and 1.5 Mbit/s downlink. Throughout Tuvalu are more than 900 subscribers who want to use the satellite service, with demand slowing down the speed of the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elearn_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-elearn-186">[186]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manu_Folau_off_Vaitupu_Harbour,_Tuvalu.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1164" data-file-width="1552" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Manu_Folau_off_Vaitupu_Harbour%2C_Tuvalu.JPG/220px-Manu_Folau_off_Vaitupu_Harbour%2C_Tuvalu.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Manu_Folau_off_Vaitupu_Harbour%2C_Tuvalu.JPG/330px-Manu_Folau_off_Vaitupu_Harbour%2C_Tuvalu.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Manu_Folau_off_Vaitupu_Harbour%2C_Tuvalu.JPG/440px-Manu_Folau_off_Vaitupu_Harbour%2C_Tuvalu.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manu_Folau_off_Vaitupu_Harbour,_Tuvalu.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Manu Folau</i> off <a href="/wiki/Vaitupu" title="Vaitupu">Vaitupu</a> atoll (2006)</div></div></div>\n<p>Transport services in Tuvalu are limited. There are about eight kilometres (5 miles) of roads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_109-5"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-109">[109]</a></sup> The streets of Funafuti were paved in mid-2002, but other roads are unpaved. Tuvalu is among <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_rail_transport_network_size" title="List of countries by rail transport network size">a few countries that do not have railroads</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a> is the only port, but there is a deep-water berth in the lagoon at <a href="/wiki/Nukufetau" title="Nukufetau">Nukufetau</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merchant_marine" title="Merchant marine">merchant marine</a> fleet consists of two passenger/cargo ships <i>Nivaga III</i> and <i>Manu Folau</i>. The <i>Nivaga III</i> and <i>Manu Folau</i> provide round-trip visits to the outer islands every three or four weeks. and travel between <a href="/wiki/Suva" title="Suva">Suva</a>, Fiji and Funafuti three or four times a year. The <i>Manu Folau</i>, a 50-metre vessel, was a gift from Japan to the people of Tuvalu. In 2015, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Development_Program" title="United Nations Development Program">United Nations Development Program</a> (UNDP) assisted the government of Tuvalu to acquire <i>MV Talamoana</i>, a 30-metre vessel that will be used to implement Tuvalu\'s <a href="/wiki/National_Adaptation_Programme_of_Action" title="National Adaptation Programme of Action">National Adaptation Programme of Action</a> (NAPA) to transport government officials and project personnel to the outer islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNDP15_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNDP15-187">[187]</a></sup> In 2015, the <i>Nivaga III</i> was donated by the government of Japan; it replaced the <i>Nivaga II</i>, which had serviced Tuvalu from 1989.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FSO0316_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FSO0316-188">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PN0507_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-PN0507-189">[189]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The only airport in Tuvalu is <a href="/wiki/Funafuti_International_Airport" title="Funafuti International Airport">Funafuti International Airport</a>. It is a tarred strip. <a href="/wiki/Fiji_Airways" title="Fiji Airways">Fiji Airways</a>, the owner of Fiji Airlines (trading as <a href="/wiki/Fiji_Link" title="Fiji Link">Fiji Link</a>) operates services three times a week (Tuesday, Thursday and Saturday) between <a href="/wiki/Suva" title="Suva">Suva</a> (originating from <a href="/wiki/Nadi" title="Nadi">Nadi</a>) and Funafuti with <a href="/wiki/ATR_72" title="ATR 72">ATR 72</a>&#8211;600, a 68-seat plane. <a href="/wiki/Air_Kiribati" title="Air Kiribati">Air Kiribati</a> service provides one flight a week to Funafuti from  <a href="/wiki/Bonriki_International_Airport" title="Bonriki International Airport">Tarawa</a> on a Wednesday.  The service uses a <a href="/wiki/Bombardier_Dash_8" title="Bombardier Dash 8">Bombardier Dash 8 100</a> series aircraft, which has the capacity to take up to 35 passengers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AK_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-AK-190">[190]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography_and_environment">Geography and environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Geography and environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Tuvalu" title="Geography of Tuvalu">Geography of Tuvalu</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Tuvalu" title="Agriculture in Tuvalu">Agriculture in Tuvalu</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tuvalu_Funafuti_atoll_beach.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2920" data-file-width="4352" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Tuvalu_Funafuti_atoll_beach.jpg/220px-Tuvalu_Funafuti_atoll_beach.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Tuvalu_Funafuti_atoll_beach.jpg/330px-Tuvalu_Funafuti_atoll_beach.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Tuvalu_Funafuti_atoll_beach.jpg/440px-Tuvalu_Funafuti_atoll_beach.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tuvalu_Funafuti_atoll_beach.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A beach at <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a> atoll.</div></div></div>\n<p>Tuvalu is a volcanic archipelago, and consists of three <a href="/wiki/Reef" title="Reef">reef</a> islands (<a href="/wiki/Nanumanga" title="Nanumanga">Nanumanga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niutao" title="Niutao">Niutao</a> and <a href="/wiki/Niulakita" title="Niulakita">Niulakita</a>) and six true <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atolls" title="Atolls">atolls</a> (<a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanumea" title="Nanumea">Nanumea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nui_(atoll)" title="Nui (atoll)">Nui</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nukufetau" title="Nukufetau">Nukufetau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nukulaelae" title="Nukulaelae">Nukulaelae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vaitupu" title="Vaitupu">Vaitupu</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PSK1_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-PSK1-191">[191]</a></sup> Its small, scattered group of atolls have poor soil and a total land area of only about 26 square kilometres (10 square miles) making it the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states_by_area" title="List of sovereign states by area">fourth smallest country in the world</a>. Over four decades, there had been a net increase in land area of the islets of 73.5&#8201;ha (2.9%), although the changes are not uniform, with 74% increasing and 27% decreasing in size. The sea level at the Funafuti tide gauge has risen at 3.9&#160;mm per year, which is approximately twice the global average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PSK2_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-PSK2-192">[192]</a></sup> The rising sea levels are identified as creating an increased transfer of wave energy across reef surfaces, which shifts sand, resulting in accretion to island shorelines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PSK1_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-PSK1-191">[191]</a></sup> although this process does not result in additional habitable land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ML_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-ML-193">[193]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a> is the largest atoll, and comprises numerous islets around a central lagoon that is approximately 25.1 kilometres (15.6 miles) (N&#8211;S) by 18.4 kilometres (11.4 miles) (W-E), centred on 179&#176;7\'E and 8&#176;30\'S. On the atolls, an annular reef rim surrounds the lagoon with several natural reef channels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> Surveys were carried out in May 2010 of the reef habitats of Nanumea, Nukulaelae and Funafuti; a total of 317 fish species were recorded during this <i>Tuvalu Marine Life</i> study. The surveys identified 66 species that had not previously been recorded in Tuvalu, which brings the total number of identified species to 607.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TMLSY_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-TMLSY-195">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TMLScR_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-TMLScR-196">[196]</a></sup> Tuvalu\'s <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_economic_zone" title="Exclusive economic zone">exclusive economic zone</a> (EEZ) covers an oceanic area of approximately 900,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJT_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-AJT-197">[197]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/2011_Tuvalu_drought" title="2011 Tuvalu drought">2011 Tuvalu drought</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tuvalu_Meteorology_Service.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Tuvalu_Meteorology_Service.jpg/220px-Tuvalu_Meteorology_Service.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Tuvalu_Meteorology_Service.jpg/330px-Tuvalu_Meteorology_Service.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Tuvalu_Meteorology_Service.jpg/440px-Tuvalu_Meteorology_Service.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tuvalu_Meteorology_Service.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Meteorological_Service" title="Tuvalu Meteorological Service">Tuvalu Meteorological Service</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fongafale" title="Fongafale">Fongafale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a> atoll</div></div></div>\n<p>Tuvalu experiences two distinct seasons, a wet season from November to April and a dry season from May to October.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CFCT_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-CFCT-198">[198]</a></sup> Westerly gales and heavy rain are the predominant weather conditions from October to March, the period that is known as <i>Tau-o-lalo</i>, with tropical temperatures moderated by easterly winds from April to November.\n</p><p>Tuvalu experiences the effects of <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Ni&#241;o">El Ni&#241;o</a> and <a href="/wiki/La_Ni%C3%B1a" title="La Ni&#241;a">La Ni&#241;a</a>, which is caused by changes in ocean temperatures in the equatorial and central Pacific. El Ni&#241;o effects increase the chances of tropical storms and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyclones" title="Cyclones">cyclones</a>, while La Ni&#241;a effects increase the chances of drought. Typically the islands of Tuvalu receive between 200 to 400&#160;mm (8 to 16&#160;in) of rainfall per month. However, in 2011, a weak La Ni&#241;a effect caused a drought by cooling the surface of the sea around Tuvalu. A <a href="/wiki/State_of_emergency" title="State of emergency">state of emergency</a> was declared on 28 September 2011, with rationing of fresh water on the islands of <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nukulaelae" title="Nukulaelae">Nukulaelae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> Households on Funafuti and Nukulaelae were restricted to two buckets of fresh water per day (40 litres).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The governments of Australia and New Zealand responded to the 2011 fresh water crisis by supplying temporary desalination plants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> and assisted in the repair of the existing desalination unit that was donated by Japan in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> In response to the 2011 drought, Japan funded the purchase of a 100 m<sup>3</sup>/d desalination plant and two portable 10 m<sup>3</sup>/d plants as part of its Pacific Environment Community (PEC) program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> Aid programs from the European Union<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10EDF_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-10EDF-210">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TWWSP_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-TWWSP-211">[211]</a></sup> and Australia also provided water tanks as part of a longer-term solution for the storage of available fresh water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RM_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-RM-212">[212]</a></sup> The La Ni&#241;a event that caused the drought ended in April&#8211;May 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> The central Pacific Ocean experiences changes from periods of La Ni&#241;a to periods of El Ni&#241;o;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA1_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA1-214">[214]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="width:75%">\n<table class="wikitable collapsible" style="width:100%; text-align:center; line-height: 1.2em;
