href="#cite_note-Weatherbase-215">[215]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_pressures">Environmental pressures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Environmental pressures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tuvalu_-_Funafuti_-_Beach.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2892" data-file-width="4352" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Tuvalu_-_Funafuti_-_Beach.jpg/220px-Tuvalu_-_Funafuti_-_Beach.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Tuvalu_-_Funafuti_-_Beach.jpg/330px-Tuvalu_-_Funafuti_-_Beach.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Tuvalu_-_Funafuti_-_Beach.jpg/440px-Tuvalu_-_Funafuti_-_Beach.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tuvalu_-_Funafuti_-_Beach.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A wharf and beach at <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a> atoll</div></div></div>\n<p>The eastern shoreline of Funafuti Lagoon  on <a href="/wiki/Fongafale" title="Fongafale">Fongafale</a> was modified during World War II when the airfield (now <a href="/wiki/Funafuti_International_Airport" title="Funafuti International Airport">Funafuti International Airport</a>) was constructed. The coral base of the atoll was used as fill to create the runway. The resulting <a href="/wiki/Borrow_pit" title="Borrow pit">borrow pits</a> impacted the fresh-water <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifer</a>. In the low-lying areas of Funafuti, the sea water can be seen bubbling up through the porous coral rock to form pools with each high tide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ML2005_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-ML2005-216">[216]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MKM_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-MKM-217">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHK1_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-AHK1-218">[218]</a></sup> In 2014, the Tuvalu Borrow Pits Remediation (BPR) project was approved so that 10 borrow pits would be filled with sand from the lagoon, leaving Tafua Pond, which is a natural pond. The New Zealand Government funded the BPR project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> The project was carried out in 2015, with 365,000 sqm of sand being dredged from the lagoon to fill the holes and improve living conditions on the island. This project increased the usable land space on Fongafale by eight per cent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During World War II, several piers were also constructed on <a href="/wiki/Fongafale" title="Fongafale">Fongafale</a> in the Funafuti Lagoon; beach areas were filled and deep-water access channels were excavated. These alterations to the reef and shoreline resulted in changes to wave patterns, with less sand accumulating to form the beaches, compared to former times; the shoreline is now exposed to wave action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TN_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-TN-221">[221]</a></sup> Several attempts to stabilise the shoreline have not achieved the desired effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The reefs at Funafuti have suffered damage, with 80 percent of the coral becoming bleached as a consequence of the increase in ocean temperatures and <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RevLT_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-RevLT-223">[223]</a></sup> The coral bleaching, which includes <a href="/wiki/Staghorn_coral" title="Staghorn coral">staghorn corals</a>, is attributed to the increase in water temperature that happened during the <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Ni&#241;o">El Ni&#241;os</a> that occurred between 1998 and 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JW_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-JW-224">[224]</a></sup> A reef restoration project has investigated reef restoration techniques;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRRP_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRRP-225">[225]</a></sup> and researchers from Japan have investigated rebuilding the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coral_reefs" title="Coral reefs">coral reefs</a> through the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Foraminifera" title="Foraminifera">foraminifera</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> The project of the Japan International Cooperation Agency is designed to increase the resilience of the Tuvalu coast against sea level rise, through ecosystem rehabilitation and regeneration and through support for sand production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JICA_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-JICA-227">[227]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The rising population has resulted in an increased demand on fish stocks, which are under stress,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RevLT_223-1"><a href="#cite_note-RevLT-223">[223]</a></sup> although the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Funafuti_Conservation_Area" title="Funafuti Conservation Area">Funafuti Conservation Area</a> has provided a fishing exclusion area to help sustain the fish population across the Funafuti lagoon. Population pressure on the resources of Funafuti, and inadequate sanitation systems, have resulted in pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krales_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krales-228">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> The Waste Operations and Services Act of 2009 provides the legal framework for waste management and pollution control projects funded by the European Union directed at organic waste composting in eco-sanitation systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TWWSP_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-TWWSP-211">[211]</a></sup> The Environment Protection (Litter and Waste Control) Regulation 2013 is intended to improve the management of the importation of non-biodegradable materials. Plastic waste is a problem in Tuvalu, for much imported food and other commodities are supplied in plastic containers or packaging.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_and_sanitation">Water and sanitation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Water and sanitation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Rainwater harvesting is the principal source of fresh water in Tuvalu. Nukufetau, Vaitupu and Nanumea are the only islands with sustainable groundwater supplies. The effectiveness of rainwater harvesting is diminished because of poor maintenance of roofs, gutters and pipes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FTMN_182-3"><a href="#cite_note-FTMN-182">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> Aid programmes of Australia and the European Union have been directed to improving the storage capacity on Funafuti and in the outer islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10EDF_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-10EDF-210">[210]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Reverse osmosis (R/O) desalination units supplement rainwater harvesting on Funafuti. The 65 m<sup>3</sup> desalination plant operates at a real production level of around 40 m<sup>3</sup> per day. R/O water is only intended to be produced when storage falls below 30%, however demand to replenish household storage supplies with tanker-delivered water means that the R/O desalination units are continually operating. Water is delivered at a cost of A$3.50 per m<sup>3</sup>. Cost of production and delivery has been estimated at A$6 per m<sup>3</sup>, with the difference subsidised by the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FTMN_182-4"><a href="#cite_note-FTMN-182">[182]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In July 2012, a United Nations Special Rapporteur called on the Tuvalu Government to develop a national water strategy to improve access to safe drinking water and sanitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> In 2012, Tuvalu developed a National Water Resources Policy under the Integrated Water Resource Management (IWRM) Project and the Pacific Adaptation to Climate Change (PACC) Project, which are sponsored by the Global Environment Fund/SOPAC. Government water planning has established a target of between 50 and 100L of water per person per day accounting for drinking water, cleaning, community and cultural activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FTMN_182-5"><a href="#cite_note-FTMN-182">[182]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tuvalu is working with the <a href="/wiki/South_Pacific_Applied_Geoscience_Commission" title="South Pacific Applied Geoscience Commission">South Pacific Applied Geoscience Commission</a> (SOPAC) to implement composting toilets and to improve the treatment of sewage sludge from septic tanks on <a href="/wiki/Fongafale" title="Fongafale">Fongafale</a>, for septic tanks are leaking into the <a href="/wiki/Lens_(hydrology)" title="Lens (hydrology)">freshwater lens</a> in the sub-surface of the atoll as well as the ocean and lagoon. Composting toilets reduce water use by up to 30%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FTMN_182-6"><a href="#cite_note-FTMN-182">[182]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cyclones_and_king_tides">Cyclones and king tides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Cyclones and king tides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cyclones">Cyclones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Cyclones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ocean_side_Funafuti.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Ocean_side_Funafuti.jpg/220px-Ocean_side_Funafuti.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Ocean_side_Funafuti.jpg/330px-Ocean_side_Funafuti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Ocean_side_Funafuti.jpg/440px-Ocean_side_Funafuti.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ocean_side_Funafuti.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ocean side of <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a> atoll showing the storm dunes, the highest point on the atoll.</div></div></div>\n<p>Because of the low elevation, the islands that make up this nation are vulnerable to the effects of <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">tropical cyclones</a> and by the threat of current and future <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">sea level rise</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krales_228-1"><a href="#cite_note-Krales-228">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMH1_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMH1-233">[233]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMH2_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMH2-234">[234]</a></sup> A warning system, which uses the <a href="/wiki/Iridium_satellite_constellation" title="Iridium satellite constellation">Iridium</a> satellite network, was introduced in 2016 in order to allow outlying islands to be better prepared for natural disasters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The highest elevation is 4.6 metres (15&#160;ft) above sea level on <a href="/wiki/Niulakita" title="Niulakita">Niulakita</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> Tuvalu thus has the second-lowest maximum elevation of any country (after the <a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a>). The highest elevations are typically in narrow storm dunes on the ocean side of the islands which are prone to overtopping in tropical cyclones, as occurred with <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Bebe" title="Cyclone Bebe">Cyclone Bebe</a>, which was a very early-season storm that passed through the Tuvaluan atolls in October 1972.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BOM1975_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-BOM1975-237">[237]</a></sup> Cyclone Bebe submerged <a href="/wiki/Funafuti" title="Funafuti">Funafuti</a>, eliminating 90% of structures on the island. Sources of drinking water were contaminated as a result of the system\'s storm surge and the flooding of the sources of fresh water.\n</p><p>George Westbrook, a trader on Funafuti, recorded a cyclone that struck Funafuti in 1883.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H83_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-H83-238">[238]</a></sup> A cyclone caused severe damage to the islands in 1894.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TAH2_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-TAH2-239">[239]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tuvalu experienced an average of three cyclones per decade between the 1940s and 1970s; however, eight occurred in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JCon_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-JCon-240">[240]</a></sup> The impact of individual cyclones is subject to variables including the force of the winds and also whether a cyclone coincides with high tides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HB72_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-HB72-241">[241]</a></sup> Funafuti\'s <a href="/wiki/Pukasavilivili" title="Pukasavilivili">Tepuka Vili Vili</a> <a href="/wiki/Islet" title="Islet">islet</a> was devastated by <a href="/wiki/1975%E2%80%9380_Southern_Hemisphere_tropical_cyclone_seasons#Cyclone_Meli" title="1975&#8211;80 Southern Hemisphere tropical cyclone seasons">Cyclone Meli</a> in 1979, with all its vegetation and most of its sand swept away during the cyclone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KFCA_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-KFCA-242">[242]</a></sup> Along with a <a href="/wiki/1989%E2%80%9390_South_Pacific_cyclone_season#Samoa_Depression" title="1989&#8211;90 South Pacific cyclone season">tropical depression</a> that affected the islands a few days later, <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Ofa" title="Cyclone Ofa">Severe Tropical Cyclone Ofa</a> had a major impact on Tuvalu with most islands reporting damage to vegetation and crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STD_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-STD-243">[243]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tuv_Disaster_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tuv_Disaster-244">[244]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Gavin" title="Cyclone Gavin">Cyclone Gavin</a> was first identified during 2 March 1997, and was the first of three tropical cyclones to affect Tuvalu during the 1996&#8211;97 cyclone season, with Cyclones <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Hina" title="Cyclone Hina">Hina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Keli" title="Cyclone Keli">Keli</a> following later in the season.\n</p><p>In March 2015, the winds and storm surge created by <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Pam" title="Cyclone Pam">Cyclone Pam</a> resulted in waves of 3 metres (9.8&#160;ft) to 5 metres (16&#160;ft) breaking over the reef of the outer islands, causing damage to houses, crops and infrastructure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TuvaluReport_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-TuvaluReport-245">[245]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RNZI19_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-RNZI19-246">[246]</a></sup> A state of emergency was declared. On Nui, the sources of fresh water were destroyed or contaminated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBS15_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-SBS15-247">[247]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RNZI14_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-RNZI14-248">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RNZI15_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-RNZI15-249">[249]</a></sup> The flooding in Nui and Nukufetau caused many families to shelter in evacuation centres or with other families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RW22_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-RW22-250">[250]</a></sup> Nui suffered the most damage of the three central islands (Nui, Nukufetau and Vaitupu);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNDP2_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNDP2-251">[251]</a></sup> with both Nui and Nukufetau suffering the loss of 90% of the crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RW30_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-RW30-252">[252]</a></sup> Of the three northern islands (Nanumanga, Niutao and Nanumea), Nanumanga suffered the most damage, with from 60 to 100 houses flooded, with the waves also causing damage to the health facility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RW30_252-1"><a href="#cite_note-RW30-252">[252]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vasafua" title="Vasafua">Vasafua</a> islet, part of the <a href="/wiki/Funafuti_Conservation_Area" title="Funafuti Conservation Area">Funafuti Conservation Area</a>, was severely damaged by Cyclone Pam. The coconut palms were washed away, leaving the islet as a sand bar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIV_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-VIV-253">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TO13_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-TO13-254">[254]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Tuvalu Government carried out assessments of the damage caused by Cyclone Pam to the islands and has provided medical aid, food as well as assistance for the cleaning-up of storm debris. Government and Non-Government Organisations provided assistance technical, funding and material support to Tuvalu to assist with recovery, including WHO, UNICEF, UNDP, OCHA, World Bank, DFAT, New Zealand Red Cross &amp; IFRC, Fiji National University and governments of New Zealand, Netherlands, UAE, Taiwan and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO04_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO04-255">[255]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="King_tides">King tides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: King tides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Tuvalu is also affected by <a href="/wiki/Perigean_spring_tide" title="Perigean spring tide">perigean spring tide</a> events which raise the sea level higher than a normal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_tide" title="High tide">high tide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup> The highest peak tide recorded by the <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu_Meteorological_Service" title="Tuvalu Meteorological Service">Tuvalu Meteorological Service</a> is 3.4 metres (11&#160;ft), on 24 February 2006 and again on 19 February 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup> As a result of the historical sea level rise, the <a href="/wiki/King_tide" title="King tide">king tide</a> events lead to flooding of low-lying areas, which is compounded when sea levels are further raised by <a href="/wiki/La_Ni%C3%B1a" title="La Ni&#241;a">La Ni&#241;a</a> effects or local storms and waves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_climate_change">Impact of climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Impact of climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_Tuvalu" title="Climate change in Tuvalu">Climate change in Tuvalu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_Tuvalu" title="Renewable energy in Tuvalu">Renewable energy in Tuvalu</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Challenges_Tuvalu_faces_as_a_result_of_climate_change">Challenges Tuvalu faces as a result of climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Challenges Tuvalu faces as a result of climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As low-lying islands lacking a surrounding shallow shelf, the communities of Tuvalu are especially susceptible to changes in sea level and undissipated storms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SS_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-SS-259">[259]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IV_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-IV-260">[260]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup> At its highest, Tuvalu is only 4.6 metres (15&#160;ft) above sea level. Tuvaluan leaders have been concerned about the effects of rising sea levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup> It is estimated that a sea level rise of 20&#8211;40 centimetres (8&#8211;16 inches) in the next 100 years could make Tuvalu uninhabitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patel_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patel-263">[263]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HJA_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-HJA-264">[264]</a></sup> A recent study published in 2018 estimated the change in land area of Tuvalu\'s nine atolls and 101 reef islands between 1971 and 2014. 75% of the islands had grown, with an overall increase in land area of more than 2%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup>.\n</p><p>Whether there are measurable changes in the sea level relative to the islands of Tuvalu is a contentious issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VG_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-VG-266">[266]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IB111213_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-IB111213-267">[267]</a></sup> There were problems associated with the pre-1993 sea level records from Funafuti which resulted in improvements in the recording technology to provide more reliable data for analysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HJA_264-1"><a href="#cite_note-HJA-264">[264]</a></sup> The degree of uncertainty as to estimates of sea level change relative to the islands of Tuvalu was reflected in the conclusions made in 2002 from the available data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HJA_264-2"><a href="#cite_note-HJA-264">[264]</a></sup> The uncertainty as to the accuracy of the data from this tide gauge resulted in a modern Aquatrak acoustic gauge being installed in 1993 by the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Meteorology" title="Bureau of Meteorology">Australian National Tidal Facility</a> (NTF) as part of the AusAID-sponsored South Pacific Sea Level and Climate Monitoring Project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JRH_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-JRH-268">[268]</a></sup> The 2011 report of the <i>Pacific Climate Change Science Program</i> published by the Australian Government,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup> concludes: "The sea-level rise near Tuvalu measured by satellite altimeters since 1993 is about 5&#160;mm (0.2&#160;in) per year."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCP2ch15_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-CCP2ch15-270">[270]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tuvalu has adopted a national plan of action as the observable transformations over the last ten to fifteen years show Tuvaluans that there have been changes to the sea levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAP_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAP-271">[271]</a></sup> These include sea water bubbling up through the porous coral rock to form pools at high tide and the flooding of low-lying areas including the airport during <a href="/wiki/Perigean_spring_tide" title="Perigean spring tide">spring tides</a> and <a href="/wiki/King_tide" title="King tide">king tides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ML2005_216-1"><a href="#cite_note-ML2005-216">[216]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MKM_217-1"><a href="#cite_note-MKM-217">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHK1_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-AHK1-218">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-272">[272]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cham_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cham-274">[274]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The atolls have shown resilience to gradual sea-level rise, with atolls and reef islands being able to grow under current climate conditions by generating sufficient sand and coral debris that accumulates and gets dumped on the islands during cyclones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PSK2014_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-PSK2014-275">[275]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWPK_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-AWPK-276">[276]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NG15_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-NG15-277">[277]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K1_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-K1-278">[278]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KTFOM2_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-KTFOM2-279">[279]</a></sup> Gradual sea-level rise also allows for coral <a href="/wiki/Polyp" title="Polyp">polyp</a> activity to increase the reefs. However, if the increase in sea level occurs at faster rate as compared to coral growth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WRD_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-WRD-280">[280]</a></sup> or if polyp activity is damaged by <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a>, then the resilience of the atolls and reef islands is less certain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PK14_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-PK14-281">[281]</a></sup> The 2011 report of <i>Pacific Climate Change Science Program</i> of Australia concludes, in relation to Tuvalu,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CFCT_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-CFCT-198">[198]</a></sup> that over the course of the 21st century:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Surface air temperatures and sea&#8209;surface temperatures are projected to continually increase (very high confidence).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCP2ch15_270-1"><a href="#cite_note-CCP2ch15-270">[270]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Annual and seasonal mean rainfalls are projected to increase (high confidence).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCP2ch15_270-2"><a href="#cite_note-CCP2ch15-270">[270]</a></sup></li>\n<li>The intensity and frequency of extreme heat days are projected to increase (very high confidence).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCP2ch15_270-3"><a href="#cite_note-CCP2ch15-270">[270]</a></sup></li>\n<li>The intensity and frequency of extreme rainfall days are projected to increase (high confidence).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCP2ch15_270-4"><a href="#cite_note-CCP2ch15-270">[270]</a></sup></li>\n<li>The incidence of drought is projected to decrease (moderate confidence).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCP2ch15_270-5"><a href="#cite_note-CCP2ch15-270">[270]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Tropical cyclone numbers are projected to decline in the south-east Pacific Ocean basin (0&#8211;40&#186;S, 170&#186;E&#8211;130&#186;W) (moderate confidence).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCP2ch15_270-6"><a href="#cite_note-CCP2ch15-270">[270]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Ocean acidification is projected to continue (very high confidence).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCP2ch15_270-7"><a href="#cite_note-CCP2ch15-270">[270]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Mean sea-level rise is projected to continue (very high confidence).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCP2ch15_270-8"><a href="#cite_note-CCP2ch15-270">[270]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/South_Pacific_Applied_Geoscience_Commission" title="South Pacific Applied Geoscience Commission">South Pacific Applied Geoscience Commission</a> (SOPAC) suggests that, while Tuvalu is vulnerable to climate change, environmental problems such as population growth and poor coastal management also affect sustainable development. SOPAC ranks the country as extremely vulnerable using the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Vulnerability_Index" title="Environmental Vulnerability Index">Environmental Vulnerability Index</a>.\n</p><p>While some commentators have called for the relocation of Tuvalu\'s population to Australia, New Zealand or <a href="/wiki/Kioa" title="Kioa">Kioa</a> in Fiji,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PIR_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-PIR-282">[282]</a></sup> in 2006 <a href="/wiki/Maatia_Toafa" title="Maatia Toafa">Maatia Toafa</a> (Prime Minister from 2004 to 2006) said his government did not regard rising sea levels as such a threat that the entire population would need to be evacuated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup> In 2013, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Enele_Sopoaga" title="Enele Sopoaga">Enele Sopoaga</a> said that relocating Tuvaluans to avoid the impact of sea level rise "should never be an option because it is self defeating in itself. For Tuvalu I think we really need to mobilise public opinion in the Pacific as well as in the [rest of] world to really talk to their lawmakers to please have some sort of moral obligation and things like that to do the right thing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RNZ030913_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-RNZ030913-285">[285]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="2015_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference_.28COP21.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2015_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference_(COP21)">2015 United Nations Climate Change Conference (COP21)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: 2015 United Nations Climate Change Conference (COP21)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Enele_Sopoaga" title="Enele Sopoaga">Enele Sopoaga</a> said at the <a href="/wiki/2015_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2015 United Nations Climate Change Conference">2015 United Nations Climate Change Conference</a> (COP21) that the goal for COP21 should be a global temperature goal of below 1.5 degrees Celsius relative to pre-industrial levels, which is the position of the <a href="/wiki/Alliance_of_Small_Island_States" title="Alliance of Small Island States">Alliance of Small Island States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASI_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-ASI-286">[286]</a></sup>\nPrime Minister Sopoaga said in his speech to the meeting of heads of state and government:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Tuvalu\'s future at current warming, is already bleak, any further temperature increase will spell the total demise of Tuvalu&#160;... For Small Island Developing States, Least Developed Countries and many others, setting a global temperature goal of below 1.5 degrees Celsius relative to pre-industrial levels is critical. I call on the people of Europe to think carefully about their obsession with 2 degrees. Surely, we must aim for the best future we can deliver and not a weak compromise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COP21ESS_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-COP21ESS-287">[287]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>His speech concluded with the plea:\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Let\'s do it for Tuvalu. For if we save Tuvalu we save the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COP21ESS_287-1"><a href="#cite_note-COP21ESS-287">[287]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The countries participating in the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Agreement" title="Paris Agreement">Paris Agreement</a> agreed to reduce their carbon output "as soon as possible" and to do their best to keep global warming "to well below 2&#160;&#176;C".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FCCC_CP_2015_L9_Rev1_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-FCCC_CP_2015_L9_Rev1-288">[288]</a></sup> Enele Sopoaga described the important outcomes of COP21 as including the stand-alone provision for assistance to small island states and some of the least developed countries for loss and damage resulting from climate change and the ambition of limiting temperature rise to 1.5 degrees by the end of the century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RNZI1515_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-RNZI1515-289">[289]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Filmography_and_bibliography">Filmography and bibliography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tuvalu&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Filmography and bibliography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<dl><dt>Filmography</dt></dl>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r853264625">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul{list-style-type:none;margin-left:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>dl>dd{margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em;text-indent:-3.2em;list-style:none}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-100{font-size:100%}</style><div class="refbegin" style="">\n<p>Documentary films about Tuvalu:\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>Tu Toko Tasi</i> (Stand by Yourself) (2000) Conrad Mill, a Secretariat of the Pacific Community (SPC) production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mill_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mill-290">[290]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a class="external text" href="http://article.wn.com/view/2015/03/15/Tuvalu_battered_by_Cyclone_Pam/" (Director: Michel Lippitsch) 34 minutes &#8211; YouTube video.</li>\n<li><i>The Disappearing of Tuvalu: Trouble in Paradise</i> (2004) by Christopher Horner and Gilliane Le Gallic.<sup Directed by Mike O\'Connor, Savana Jones-Middleton and Wayne Tourell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WT_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-WT-292">[292]</a></sup></li>\n<li><i>Going Under</i> (2004) by Franny Armstrong, Spanner Films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MKM_217-3"><a href="#cite_note-MKM-217">[217]</a></sup></li>\n<li><i>Before the Flood: Tuvalu</i> (2005) by Paul Lindsay (Storyville/BBC Four).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MKM_217-4"><a href="#cite_note-MKM-217">[217]</a></sup></li>\n<li><i>Time and Tide</i> (2005) by Julie Bayer and Josh Salzman, Wavecrest video.</li>\n<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=R7GI7zeHOxQ" rel="nofollow"><i>Tuvalu: Renewable Energy in the Pacific Islands Series</i></a> (2012) a production of the
